RATING: 10
Finally found a city-building game that I really enjoy. It feels thematic all the way through, and the interactions between tiles make sense. Everything pulls together to form a game that rewards good strategic and tactical play. With so many tiles available, it offers good re-playability. The association of tiles with those in other suburbs provides some form of interaction between players, and is pretty thematic too. I really feel like I'm building a suburb in this game -- brilliant design!

RATING: 6
After the ingenuity of the marketplaces in Glen More and Peloponnes (two very similar tile-laying games), Suburbia feels simplistic, derivative and a little bland. The theme is fairly well integrated, but a city-building game seems to me to require development over time of each individual tile (either enhancement, decay, or production abilities). The graphic design is poor and adds to making the game a very dry experience. However, with all that said, this sub-category of the tile-laying genre (whereby tiles are directly affected by those placed adjacent to them) tends to produce an enjoyable experience regardless, and Suburbia is no exception. It's simple to learn, with pretty deep strategies involved. Incorporating the fashionable-right-now variable winning conditions (Kingdom Builder) and secret objectives (Troyes, Archipelago) seems a little forced, but I guess it will prevent one over-riding strategy from winning every game. If this is your first foray into the genre, it's a fine game. If you've already played Glen More or Peloponnes, you don't really need to consider Suburbia.

RATING: 7
Combine the combos is the name of the game. I find buying and laying the tiles rather enjoyable. Every turn you are trying to create the best combos while gathering momentum for your income and victory point generation so that you do not run out of steam too early. Furthermore, the common and secret goals give you one more thing to ponder.

Some negatives can be found as well. My primary whine is about the tiles that give you bonuses for every tile of certain type in the game. It is just so hard to keep track of what everyone else has. Most of the time you also forget to take the benefit when other players add tiles into their boroughs. Ok, this introduces interaction into the game but at the cost of playability. Other issues I have with the art which is rather uninspiring. However, this is in the eye of the beholder.

The similarities with Castles of Mad King Ludwig are quite obvious. Personally, I prefer the Castle building due to its art and the more limited cascade of things triggered when you place the tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.97
Quickly ascending to become one of my favorite gateway Euros.  A nice tile-laying 'Sim City' type of game, where you build your own suburb to hopefully meet the requirements of one or more end-game goals (or you can completely ignore the end game goals and try to just get points in other ways--though I've never seen a player emerge victorious that way).  

While the game looks a little dry, it actually is quite interesting how the theme has been incorporated into many of the tiles (e.g., the hotel will give you benefits based on the residential tiles your neighbors have built, while the resort will give you benefits based on all of the residential tiles in the game--after all, why would someone go to a hotel in their own neighborhood?).

There is a fair amount of interaction between tiles played in your own suburb and tiles that other players place in their suburbs, so I purchased some of those nifty metal cubes out of the Geek Store so that people can mark tiles in their suburb that are affected by non-adjacent tiles in their own suburb or tiles in other suburbs to remind them to not forget the related benefits.  It can be a bit unruly otherwise to track all of the various movements until you've played a few times.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Just a excellent game. Suburbia is a sim city/suburb game that involves drafting hexagonal tiles into your growing suburb. The tiles trigger powers when placed adjacent to other tiles and you want to build synergies that compliment your play and help you achieve hidden and public objectives. Of course, you also need to pay attention to other players and sometimes purchase tiles to block them. So the game is not just a multiplayer solitaire. the score track is also an interesting mechanic because you are penalized as you progress which you need to account for. This keeps you from growing unchecked and affords other players a chance to keep up. Suburbia contains a lot of interesting and I dare say relatively novel/uncommon mechanics that really help it both stand out and be a good game. It was designed by the same person who did castles of mad King Ludwig (very different game), and is a Mensa select. As far as I am concerned, suburbia adds credibility to the Mensa select award. Bravo bezier games.

RATING: 5
A really cool game that suffers from some pretty debilitating flaws.  Love the mechanics and implementation.  Works very well.  Why the not-love?  Some of the goals are awful as common goals.  Streakiness of buildings (how many of each are included) is horrendous.  And there's no easy fix because it's a core game mechanic.  If you get lucky and invest in x tile when all of the parts for its engine are in the game, good for you!  Otherwise you lose.  Oh well!  The lack of being able to predict what your engine should be is wrong.  Also, a couple tiles are too powerful, or can be too powerful in the wrong hands (i.e., my opponents').  I want to like this one (really!), but there's too much not-quite-right with it and my enjoyment is hindered.  Ah well!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I played this game just twice, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

I didn't dislike Suburbia, but I also didn't enjoy it as much as other people seem to do. There're some aspects of the game that bother me and made the experience not so fun. First is the real estate market mechanism. It happens a lot that I couldn't get what I needed just because of the way the tiles came out, because, when the turn came back to me, that tile was gone. Often also the second choice was gone and that left me with some suboptimal choice that I was forced to take. That is just too random for me and simply isn't fun. Even more than that is the end game situation. While I appreciate the unpredictable end game trigger, at the end of the game I feel like I didn't get where I wanted to go with my strategy. I'm just entering that area of enjoyment for what I built and achieved, that the game finishes. That deprives me of the sense of accomplishment that other games about building give me. Another minor complaint is the chaos that often comes from the tile activation. It happens easily to forget to apply some effect and that's annoying. Although this probability disappears with experience.

Overall Suburbia is an ok game, that failed to meet my expectations. For sure I understand why people like it so much, but some of its design choices just don't work for me.

RATING: 9.5
Suburbia is the closest thing to SimCity in a box I've played. The strategic depth to rules complexity ratio is pretty high, meaning it's quick to learn, but hard to master. I love that the only randomness in the game, what tiles enter the market, is mitigated by the high initial market price of those tiles. I like how tile placement and city planning are crucial to success, just as in real (sub)urban development. The small amount of player interaction is exactly right for this near-solitaire optimization game. The tile interaction follows real world NIMBY/YIMBY development principles. AP is limited by the tiles being face down until they enter the market and players can think in advance during other players' turns. I am always eager to play this with anyone who has the time.

RATING: 3
My rating was an 8 before I played my second game, with the only thing stopping it from being a 9 was the rather bland visual style. I want you to think about that and realize how wildly inconsistent the experience of this game is going to be.

Suburbia wants to be an engine-builder but doesn't want to take responsibility for actually achieving the polish that those types of games require. In order to have a good engine-builder, you want a little bit of random chance to spice the game up. And Suburbia does this. Except that in this game (at least the 2P variant), you can easily get to a point where the +$10 slot is the only one anyone buys out of, if they built their money engines correctly, which we did. Unfortunately, this means the player who wins is the one who picks up PR Firm first since it's completely broken, stopping you from losing any rep for the rest of the game as you pass red tape. This is the single most game-changing effect, since red tape is supposed to slow your VP-gaining. Casino WISHES it was this good.

For some reason they appear to not have play-tested the possibility that players just ignore the trade row and only buy Heavy Factories, which has proven in our games to be a first-order-optimal strategy. Which of course means it happens every game, so you have all these dumb as fuck rounds early game where every player wants their factory tower. Which sucks because now you're blazing through red tape. I could see some groups never learning about this, but once you do, it kinda kills the whole game. Yellow tiles can do everything and rarely punish players (unlike Blue tiles which get punished hard).

Pure population in early game is suicide, since spamming through red tape just means other people with properly built engines will overtake you. This of course means early Green tiles tend to get ignored by everyone.

Early money ramp and gain is pretty much the only strategy left and is indeed the same flow the game takes every time: ramp money early so you can buy expensive broken tiles mid (Casino+PR Firm), then double anything that gives a fucktillion population right before the game ends. If you're spending doubling tokens before the last 3 rounds, you fucked up (or got sacked because this game is completely unbalanced).

With practically zero strategy left, now it's time to move on to tactics. Are there any? Well, sure once you get some money ramp going, you can start trying to make good formations and plan for end game scoring. Except not if you took a restaurant strategy and hardly any were randomly chosen to be in your game. Or airports. Or parking lots. Or schools. Jesus fuck, what is this game's goddamn problem? Why is EVERYTHING chosen at random? You pick 15 random A tiles, 15 random B tiles, 15 random C tiles, shuffle them each randomly, add the End Tile RANDOMLY into pile C, and then hope to absolute shit you don't get fucked.

Which leads me to the travesty that is the end tile. In what fucking instance of a strategy game is it okay to just suddenly and without any warning END THE GAME? None. Absolutely none. That isn't how skill works. That's how blind luck works. You have this nice tile-laying puzzle to solve with a cute drafting mechanic and all these interlocking piec--OH NOPE, WAIT, TIME TO STOP PLAYING. There could be 14 fucking tiles left to go but it's time to stop. This is an absolutely abhorrent tile and we had to leave it out just to have a small amount of fun.

On top of that, for a 2Player game, 15 tiles in each pile feels too short. Engine-builders should definitely end before they wear out their welcome but Suburbia has a huge problem where the exponential growth can happen well before it's even possible to end the game. So if you then also make the end random, it feels like you're just fucking players who could make a comeback (and have when the tile was removed). Actually, with 17 tiles in each stack, the 2P game felt the correct length. This just screams "not playtested" to me.

I could rant for eternity about the bad with this game. The good: component quality is nice, could be comfy if all the randomness calms the fuck down for like three minutes.

Overall, a great idea wrapped in a mess of bad mechanics.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I suppose the mechanics do tie in with the theme; but it doesn't prevent Suburbia from being a fairly dry, non-immersive game. The utter dreariness of the drab art doesn't help at all, either.

A remarkably fiddly game, and the tiny icons just add to the fiddlyfest. While Suburbia is not a luck-driven game, luck-of-the-draw DOES play a huge part with regards to the individual goals that a player can strive for: many of the goal targets cancel each other out, and players can be in a boring Mexican stand-off with one another.

I see clearly why there are speed-bumps in the VP track, since there is a most definite runaway leader problem in Suburbia...even with the speed bumps!

A very good redeeming quality about Suburbia, though, is how utterly different each game will be.

RATING: 6
So here we are again, building a city and finding that it is better to build parks than heavy industries next to residential neighbourhoods.  Suburbia gives each player their own city to develop rather than a shared space, then introduces a bunch of endgame bonuses and in-game calculations to make you pay attention to your neighbours.  Then there's a market in which you can pay a premium for that desirable tile, or wait in the hope that no opponent will buy it as its price reduces.  Another familiar element (from Age of Steam) is the reductions in income and reputation at various points of population growth.

So Suburbia throws together familiar elements in a fairly novel assemblage.  However, I found it only moderately engaging to play. Get sufficient income, grow population, pay close attention to the end game bonuses, that's all isn't it?

I found Suburbia graphically bland, though clear and functional.  The money board that isn't big enough for the pile of money appears the only misstep in a well put together game.  The rules are clear and seem complete.

RATING: 7
This game plays in a nice mellow way such that the fun is in buying things and adding them onto your tableau to improve your engine.  It might be a good "go to" game if you wanted a nice mellow engine building game with tile laying.

I think the one thing perhaps lacking from this game is a sense of adrenaline or excitement, and curiously, I think that may be in part be attributable to the spartan art chosen.  Part of the excitement in any game involving a shopping component is the feeling of REALLY wanting something.  It helps a player experience that "want" more deeply if the object of your desire looks really great.  (For example, ani-meeples in Agricola do "work" better.)

Here, the art is, temperature-wise, almost sub-zero, faded and abstract. There is thus, less emotional desire for any item, and one is left essentially with wanting something purely based upon its intellectual "gaming effect."  Nothing absolutely wrong with that - such is the case in all abstracts - but it feels like a thematic and emotional opportunity was missed with the art in this game.

For first timers, of course, some tiles are fiddly with some huge questions of tile interpretation swinging the game. Then again, Castles of Burgundy had this same problem, and I readily forgave it.  You just have to play it enough times I guess.

RATING: 8
The best thing about this game, in my opinion, are the goals. You draw which you personally try to achieve (draw 1,discard 1) and those that you're competing to achieve with your opponents (also randomly drawn). There is player interaction (you have to consider the affect of the tiles you draw on the other players) and the fact you can try to take or remove tiles that would be beneficial to your opponent. There's definitely some randomness. Another problem I have with the game is the goals (which is one of the best things about the game) it's almost too clear which tiles you should pick, because your goals should focus your strategy (although to some people, it may not seem clear). Lastly, the interaction between the hexagons you place in your city is pretty necessary in the game design, nonetheless, how you should place the tiles is very straightforward I think, kind of simplistic.

RATING: 7.745
Suburbia is a great game. I love the process of trying to find the right tiles to buy to activate different abilities in your suburb. Finding those synergies is incredibly fun to me. The delicate balancing act that comes with increasing your income, your reputation, and the setbacks that come with increased population are a ton of fun. I love engine building games, and this is an engine building game that also incorporates finances and a theme that I enjoy. This is absolutely one of my favorite games.

Update: I really cooled on this one a lot over the years. I still enjoy it and think it is fun. However, as I've moved toward heavier games, I've become less satisfied with the simple buy a tile place a tile simple decision turns in this game. I'm still happy to play this, but I don't need to own it anymore.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.4
Initial rating, based on playing once. My very first reaction upon seeing this game was instant love. I still think the theme and overall cohesion is awesome, but gameplay is too fiddly and unforgiving for my taste, with there being far too many points for mistakes. 

I particularly dislike the reality of tiles which have an effect based on any other tile of a certain type being played anywhere by any player. If you have multiple of these in your suburb, the game becomes a boring slog of paying far too much attention to far too many tiny details. Having "oh sorry, I forgot to adjust [this]" moments in a game is okay every so often, but from about the midgame, Suburbia has this nearly every turn. Preventing this means slow turns (re-read all your tiles again), which have to slow other players down too, because if they don't wait until you've looked at all your tiles, they'll make a move which could've had an effect on you without you noticing.

Newbie mistakes (not building up a money engine) are also punished too severely (no good way to recover).

RATING: 5
Normally city-building, engine-building, and auctions are mechanics that I love... but suburbia's implementation creates an average experience rather than something impressive/outstanding. Why? There is simply too much luck. Now normally, auction mechanics can eliminate the swingyness of a game while adding a decision. But towards the end of the game, certain tiles are a no-brainer and the auction track doesn't extend far enough. Combine that with an uncertain end-game, with a set-up that randomly adds/removes tiles from the game, and with hidden stretch goals that are VERY influential... the game feels less calculating and more of one where players are hoping that a tile does/doesn't come out. 

But it is very easy to learn, plays smoothly, and does allow for some creative decisions. As long as you don't take the game too seriously, it is a good/average game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
First outing: Surburbia felt like Through the Ages light without the direct and negative player interaction, which in no bad thing.  Goal tiles apart, the rules were easy to follow.  The graphics were functional and clear, except for the confusing use of a similar blue for both the civic and the lake tiles.  The game felt quite polished and well developed as I’d expect from both Ted and Lookout.

However we had one big and unnecessary issue regarding the goal tiles.  Was this game blind play tested?  A whole page is devoted to reproducing the goal tiles full size.  The images should have been much smaller and coupled with an explanatory narrative which was badly needed.  For example, in our three player game the Miscreant tile was drawn as a public tile.  It was a close game.  We each thought we had won.  The whole game hinged on the definition of this tile.  Player ‘H’ read it (incorrectly we agreed after post match analysis) that the winner on the tile was the player with the lowest reputation.  Player ‘W’ read it as being the player with the most (biggest negative) ‘immediate impact’ to reputation icons, player ‘B’ read it at the player with the least (lowest negative) ‘conditional impact’ icons.  We eventually resolved the game and the winner using a definition of most (biggest negative) conditional impact icons including the effect on the adjacent tiles.  So, a three plus hour game came down to a rules discussion, which with an explanation in the rules, would have been rendered unnecessary.  Not very satisfactory.

On the plus side, we all enjoyed the game.  What I would recommend is, that for all of the public goals at least, the players agree what they mean before starting to play.  I have ignored this issue in deciding my rating as it is a game that I expect explore further and to get my money’s worth from.  Qualified congratulations to T&T.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Suburbia is a very enjoyable and thematic tile-laying game, in which each player builds his own neighborhood/tableau.

The gameplay is very straightforward: each turn you buy a tile and add it to your tableau (triggering various effects) or "invest" in one of your tiles doubling its effects (up to three times per game).

Reading the rules I feared it would be fiddly to track down the various effect, but everything flows quite smoothly.

Gameplay is not very interactive (you build your own tableau), but some tiles affect other player's neighborhoods, so everyone is involved even on others' turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Had this game on my wishlist for some time and finally received it as a Christmas gift in 2014.

After 12 months I've played it 14 times and still like to play it regularly.
The game offers a nice mix of strategical and tactical decisions. Some buildings act as triggers with adjacent tiles, your borough or even your opponents' boroughs. This creates some nice interaction between players and adds (dis-)incentive to build certain buildings.

You get more tiles than you use per game thus the game offers good replay-value. Further more the tiles have different relative value depending on the number of players (e.g. buildings with triggered beneficial effects are certainly more valuable the more player there are to trigger them).

RATING: 9
Suburbia is amazing. In much the same way Flashpoint creates a realistic fire-fighting atmosphere, Suburbia gives the impression that players are actually building bustling cities. Very few games balance a reward-penalty decision-making process as well, from deciding how much to pay for a tile, to where to place it on your grid. There are very few perfect choices, making each turn an incredibly deep and thought-provoking experience. Can't recommend this game enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoy Suburbia quite a bit. It is about as streamlined as a city-builder can be. Each turn, you can buy a tile and add it to your city. The tile will give an immediate bonus (increase income or Reputation) and then it might give a bonus to other tiles around it, and then tiles around the tile might give bonuses. Sounds complicated, but it really isn't. I like how the game makes you go for a balanced strategy by making you lower your income and Reputation tracks many times during the game as you get VP's. Goals add a public as well as a secret thing to shoot for to give extra points at games end. A solid game that should stay in my collection for many years.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I used to love SimCity, and often I've thought about tracking down a good city-building board game to scratch that same itch. Well this is the closest I've seen so far. The mechanisms are simple, but with the number of things you have to think about it might not fly with non-gamers. The progression of the building prices seems logical, but perhaps a little too steep. I ran into the same problem with this game that I deal with in all engine-building games. I focus too much on the final goal and forget to build a good foundation, so I end up desperate for cash by the end. It is nice that there is always something you can do, though to grab cash by placing a lake. The goals will probably be the hot-button issue with this game. I liked them because even a player like myself, who was scrounging for cash most of the game, can still compete for the win if you accomplish the majority of the goals. Others will dislike this because they will feel it doesn't necessarily reward the player who built the best city. Most of the mechanisms make thematic sense, which I like, but I'm still not sold because it would probably stymie my parents.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
One of my favorite games. Basically the board game version of sim city. 

Buy tiles from a market and place them in your city then calculate the effect of that tile- very simple mechanics but some complexity in what tiles do and in strategy. 

2 expansions are optional and not super necessary

Lengthy and complete tutorial, most rules are explained in the first 10 minutes: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=H6rn4nYkauo

Super fast tutorial: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tbvQyeDVIRQ
Error: They made some mistakes- you get 2 secret goals and you only keep one (not both), and only you can score points from it, not anyone else

RATING: 7
Kind of hated my first play but has grown on me a little in subsequent plays. There are lots of "retroactive" effects in this game, e.g. a tile that gives you +2 when you build on a lake also gives you +2 for everything you've already constructed on a lake. This can be frustrating at first as an error in counting up your incomes is often very, very tricky to reverse.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Manages to be pretty simple and still fairly deep and thematic which is quite a feat.  The city building theme is cool and is integrated well the game plays pretty quickly.  The goals can make the game a little weird, depending on what comes up.  At the end of one game we had everyone trying to get rid or their money (spendthrift), create lakes (aquatic engineer), and lose reputation (miscreant).  I like this kind of chaos but having the game hinge on these kinds of things is a little strange, at least thematically.  Also, it's a little like a mathy optimization problem with several things to track and lots of cube moving.  It would be nice to have a computer manage all the this stuff- a similar feeling as I got from Sid Meier's Civ the board game (FFG).  Yeah the Civ game and the Sim City games seem like they'd work better on a computer- funny huh?  Anyway, this is quicker and more streamlined than FFG Civ and the book keeping, while fiddly (push cubes, the cube crossed a line, push other cubes, etc) is manageable.  Another negative is that the components are ugly, they definitely chose function over form, which is good, but maybe a little more aesthetics would have been nice.  In the end, I didn't think it really lived up to the hype, though I would play it again, I found it a little too puzzle-y and fiddly.

RATING: 5
Played this last night for the first time.  Not a bad City building game.  It's quick to teach and fairly quick to play (I think our 4-player with 3 noobs took a little over an hour).  There's probably a bit of depth to it, but it didn't jump out at me.  I'll happily play it again and it's possible I'd get it as a medium-light next-step up from gateway game.  However, nothing particularly innovative here.  I still wish City Tycoon had better components.  The game itself was much stronger than Suburbia; however, Suburbia is definitely more approachable and has MUCH better bits.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
We played the game for the first time last night.

For the first half of the game I really enjoyed the whole thing... well themed, fairy good pacing. Love the feeling of building a town, and the rules are simple. Great! I had developed a relatively solid lead in both money and points to the start of the C round.

But then...

The "PR firms" came out early on and one player obtained both of them. He quickly pegged 15 points/turn and won by a landslide. I obtained an early "casino" tile which gave me effectively limitless money for the rest of the game and came 2nd.

I'm sorry, but the "PR firm" (particularly) and the "casino" tiles are ridiculously overpowered. They completely ruin the game if they come out early and are not burned. They remove the whole "resource management" aspect of the game (which, er, is the whole game).

Pretty much, if any player (a) has money saved up heading into the C stack and (b) is lucky enough to flip the tile on their turn, they win the game.

It alone soured the entire experience of the game for our group -- including the winner.

I'm not sure what on earth the designers were thinking.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow! I don't think I've ever seen a game whose theme is so fully integrated into its mechanics as Suburbia. The balance between building reputation and income vs. facing the pros and cons of an ever-increasing population is nearly perfect. 

During our first game there were some moments where we actually stopped playing and paused to talk about the cleverness of the game's design. The moment I was forced to place my housing projects in a corner of my board behind the factories and garbage dumps, I knew that this was an amazingly thematic and brilliantly thought-out game. By the end, we all had something resembling real suburbs, with residential neighborhoods, office parks, and warehouse districts, all of which were shaped by the mechanics of the game. On a design level, one of the best games I've played.

[b]TOTAL = 416.5/50 = 8.33[/b]


:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Nice tile-drafting game with a ressources management and engine-building aspect to it, with a really appealing "Sim City" theme, that is well implemented into most of the tiles abilities.
++ There are really a lot of strategies explore in this game as there is a lot of possibilities and combinaisons to score points (you can focus on different type of building according to the public objectives and your private objective).
++ I like that you have to manage the spacial element of the tiles in your borough to respect some criteria (proximity from other type of tiles to combo with them) which for the most part feel thematic, and also that you have to check your opponent's borough for other bonuses on other tiles.
++ I really like the more streamlined mechanisms of this versus the newer Castles of Mad King Ludwig (CoMKL), because it give a better flow here: Analyse your tableau and your opponent one, choose a tiles, place it, and score it (the tile, any adjacency and any other bonuses/penalties).
++ Compared to CoMKL, I like that the setup is faster in this one.
++ Compared to CoMKL, I like that the scoring is more straightforward and thus faster in this one.
++ (App version only) The solo campaign is fun and challenging and force us to play differently to achieve the different city's goals.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- (Physical version only) I would still find this game a bit too fiddly and with a lot of upkeep, as we always have to calculate every points/income/reputation we do from the tile itself, then any adjacency with other tiles and then any other bonuses (or penalties) from any other tiles and this, also from your opponents tiles in some cases. The App obviously really help with that and the information is really well presented to quickly see the bonuses and penalties of every placement.

Number of Player = 1-4
Online game length = 20 min.
2-player length = Approx. 60 min.
Player Count Preference = Best at 2 or 3, recommended at 4.  :thumbsup::thumbsup:
[/b]

RATING: 7
Suburbia is an engine building game, which is pretty much my favorite type of game, so it's no surprise I liked it.

On your turn, you usually buy a tile and add it to your town.  Most tiles either increase your cities income (which you'll use to buy more tiles) or increase the rate at which people move to your city (which are your points).  Almost all the tiles interact with each other in some way, so you're always watching how your group your tiles, or watching what other players are doing since some tiles get bonuses for what others are doing.

You also have to build your city carefully, as the more people that move to your city, the harder the game makes it to keep your income and population increases flowing.  If you're not careful, you can eventual start losing people and money!

At the end of the game, your score is your population, plus whatever goals you win.  There are a few public goals, and each player has a private goal.  Goals are stuff like having the most grey buildings, having the highest income, or building the fewest lakes.  The goals actually score a fair number of points, so they're important to keep in mind when building!

Overall, a cool engine building game with a fair amount of interaction.  I look forward to playing more!

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play on the iOS app with 2 (real!) players:

Okay so the app is not really the greatest way to learn this. The game took much longer than it ought to just because certain things didn't really click for us until partway through the game.

I'm coming at this having played Castles of Mad King Ludwig first, and this is an enjoyable counterpart. There's definitely a LOT more calculation going on here - to be honest I found the amount of math in this game a little paralyzing (this is where the app version wins!). I could see this inducing some SERIOUS AP in people.

This game is a nice mix of tile laying and engine building, with a couple of interesting unique mechanics thrown in. I really like the way lakes work - it's nice to generally always have a move, but adding that extra layer of interaction where you can use them for tile denial (if you can afford it!) is a great little extra choice point. The investment mechanic might be my favorite part though - it's a great little decision, and getting to trash something from the market afterward is just icing on the cake.

I wouldn't hesitate to recommend this game even though I think I personally prefer Ludwig. But I also think these two can happily coexist.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This game would probably work much better as a computer game as the upkeep is tedious as well as the constant calculations and checks to see if you’ve taken every tile into account. I’ve heard that they will make this game as an iPad app which is going to take just 10-15 minutes to play (which should be compared to stated 90 minutes here on BGG)…

In any case, Suburbia has a lot of good stuff going on. I’m a big fan of games that include a spatial element. The way you’re placing your tiles and thinking about how to increase their benefits is something I enjoy a lot. How to maximize the synergy between the tiles is basically the game. There are lots of different tiles so the variety in each game is huge. However, I also think this is a downside to the game; you’re never sure which tiles are in the game, so if you’re planning to go for e.g. airports or green buildings etc., you can get screwed if none (or perhaps just a few) shows up. This is quite critical I think. 

I like the various and different goals which you have access to at the beginning of the game and which help you shape your strategy. I’m also a sucker for secret goals, so that’s always nice and it’s often hard to figure out what goal a specific opponent has, which is as it should be so you cannot just copy their moves. I also like that you need to balance between your population and income, though income is vastly more important at the beginning of the game! It’s clever that it becomes more and more expensive as you get more and more points. 

Maybe it makes sense that certain tiles provide income or loses reputation etc. depending on which tiles you put them next to, and while I can appreciate this thematic touch it doesn’t mean anything special to me as I don’t really care about thematic flavor. The artwork on the tiles and the rest of the game is quite bland. 
Finally I will state that the game is very fiddly; you constantly need to check to see if your own tiles provide any income/reputation – and then check if your newly placed tile is affected by the tiles in your opponents’ boroughs. This leads me to think that a SimCity-kind-of-boardgame perhaps just doesn’t work as a boardgame. It’s a decent game that I would play again, but I don’t need to own it.

Yo dogg, I heard you like Carcassonne and Race For the Galaxy so I put a Carcassonne in your Race For The Galaxy.

Tableau building game with a strong spatial element. Puzzly multi-player solitaire up to 11. Evokes its city-planning theme w/o simulating city planning -- don't go in expecting SimCity the board game if you want to not have it lose you straightaway. Rules are extremely tight and well-presented, but working out the various adjacency bonuses and penalties seems like a massive pain in the booty; fortunately the iPad version takes care of that for you, so that may actually be the preferred format in my eyes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game requires the player to exclusively concentrate on income early in the game to build a economic engine.  Maximising your population is emphasised in the end game since this is the core scoring element.  This means that what could be considered a good tile at the beginning and end of the game can be a small subset of the tiles available in the market.  This can make selecting a tile trivial if you can afford it and equally leave you with no good options on another ocassion.

Most of the games I have played so far have been two player (very enjoyable) but I was expecting this game to be better with three players this not my experience.  

If you do not get access to a significant share of income enhancing tiles through the market in early game you will simply be out of the running.  In the three player game the increased demand for appropriate and timely tiles in the market mean that someone will probably have a bad start and will have no chance to catch up (assuming equally skilled players).  The three player game is a little long to be flagging up a loss so early.

This good game that could have been a great game if there was a way to make lemonade whem the tile market is handing you lemons.  Equally a catch up mechanism may have flattened out the issue a little (I was thinking about power grid).

As a quick two player game this downside is less of an issue.

I am playing this on IOS at the moment and the downside is still there but less of an issue (its not going to put you out as much if a few of the games you are playing simultaneously is giving you no breaks).

RATING: 8
Pleasantly surprised! Gameplay options on your turn are dirt simple. 90% of the time you simply choose a tile, pay for it, and place it. You get to build your own 'burb, and the key is that relative locations for many things matter. And it's mostly in a logical way. Bonus points for playing well [i]under[/i] the rated time. I have no idea how this could take 90 minutes. One would not be blamed for considering this multiplayer solitaire, but there's not enough incentive to pay attention to what others are doing to make it interactive. But it will never be confused for a highly interactive game. But that's fine, not all games need to be. There are way more tiles than you need for a single game, which means lots of replayability. It also means a fair bit of randomness because you don't know what will or won't come out. This handily stuffs and fires Urban Sprawl. As if it needed firing. The only knock against it is that as players' cities grow, it can be [i]very[/i] to overlook an adjustment that needs to be made, as many buildings start interacting with each other. So you have to really be paying attention, not just to what you should do, but what the bookkeeping required is for you what you [i]actually[/i] do.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Games with adjacency mechanisms continue to hold my interest, and so when I was in a position to play Suburbia, I was very glad to be able to do so: the game had succeeded in avoiding my attention for a while now. Unfortunately the initial acquiantance was not as pleasurable as I'd imagined. Now this can be attributed in part to me playing the game with just 2, where I gather that the performance isn't all that great. Matters become a truly bland min-maxing and just politely ignoring the other player: chances are far greater that any of the tiles on offer will benefit you more than the loss caused by withholding the opponent something. With more players there is a quicker rotation and thus some forcing to make the choice in your disadvantage. But that wasn't the biggest problem, really: I found Suburbia to be a very calculative game. There is no visual feedback as to how well you're doing, or some other player, for that matter. There is also no easy way to see what an opponent is up to: the tiles are, from afar, bland, and the interesting bit is small text which is hard to read upside down. And on top of that there are tiles which affect and are affected by those already placed, including over at other players' domains! Crikey. Talk about creating complex tile interactions for no good reason! All in all the game was a bit of a letdown. I might play again with more players, but to be honest I'm not holding my breath for the occasion: it will come out of Glen More's table time, after all.

RATING: 6
Reminds me of SimCity somewhat. This is a tile-laying game where you are building up your own spearate cities and trying to manage various types of buildings (commercial, residential, industrial, and government) to yield you the best result. You have to manage your income, reputation, and population throughout the game. The bigger your population gets, the more difficult it is to manage your reputation and income levels. Placing tiles typically give you an immediate benefit but will occasionally affect previously-placed tiles on both your city and your opponent's city. This game is well-worth your time.

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Enjoyable game that's very easy to get to the table, but with 2 minor niggles. Production quality is not up to standard, several pieces have visible tears on them. The game also features a lot of goals for achieving the "least" of something at the end of the game. Whilst I understand the need for them, they aren't well implemented and you can wind up with very negative situations when two players are competing for a particular least goal, if they tie the players score nothing at all. With the "Most" goals you are at least developing your town and board position, but the least goals mean that you are shutting off options, so you often end up in a mutually self destructive showdown, handing the victory to the person who did not engage, It's a very negative equilibrium as well, because you've usually damaged your board position enough that pulling away won't help, and by doing so, you give free points to the person you were tying with. We've houseruled so that we remove half of the least goals before dealing out, but I feel it was an oversight to make the goals 50/50 Least/Most

Edit: Played this with the expansion and our variant rule where we put half of the least goals back in the box and this game was suddenly very fun again. The addition of bonus and challenge tiles have improved the game dramatically. In the base game, it's conceivably possible to achieve all or most of the public goals and your private goal. With the challenge/bonus tiles, that is no longer the case. So it's a matter of picking your battles. In addition the rewards you gain for each are no longer directly equivalent, so it's a matter of judgement which you pursue. The borders are also incredibly fun. They add a slightly wacky special element to the puzzle that really changes the experience.

RATING: N/A
City Building, Competitive
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
For those familiar with Sim City the video game, Suburbia has a similar feel as you try and grow your borough while keeping several factors in balance (income, reputation and population).  Players acquire a hexagonal tile each round and adjust their figures accordingly, with previously placed tiles having potential influence as well.

On the plus side, this game is instantly engrossing, and having player-specific goals to work toward are smart.  There are enough tiles that each game is definitely unique, so the replay value here is high.

The main drawback is downtime - this is a game that really encourages AP, so in a 4-player game you spend too much time waiting around.  (Yes, there are a few player interactions to watch for.)  A minor complaint on the production - the rules and tile description sheets are very light/flimsy.

On the whole, a great game that I know is going to get played regularly in our group.

RATING: 8.4

Definite recommendation!

RATING: 8
Fairly simply game from a mechanics/rules perspective, but can lead to some really complicated calculations when it comes not only to your own decision trees, but your opponents as well if you're trying to stay on top of the kind of tiles you need to deny them. Anyway, it's a really good game that I personally don't feel the need to own for the time being because I have access to it via my main gaming group. If I get to the point where I want to play it with my family, I might pick up a copy. But it's definitely something everyone in the hobby should try at least once.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
The designer clearly borrowed from Through the Ages here. unfortunately, the lighter game with a spatial mechanic and city-building theme doesn't compare to the game by which it was influenced. The end game scoring itself just ruins the game. 

Purchasing tiles and placing them in your burough in order to benefit from them. Spatial in the sense that where you place it relative to other tiles will affect your income or reputation. Score is population plus bonus points for specific goals at game end (goals change from game to game). Negative seems to be that start player is a pretty significant disadvantage with no benefit at all. End-game goals (most of x, or fewest of x) score between 10 and 20 points each, and ties go to no one. Three of these goals are face up for all players from the beginning of the game, but each player has one "secret" goal that only he knows about (but can still be earned by any player).

RATING: 7.5
Tough to rate this one.

It definitely has a unique sort of puzzle aspect to it and the ideas, mechanics and tiles are nice. But it also has--at best--only moderate player interaction.

It seems that the end-game bonuses might occasionally lead to a bit of a broken ending. One person's 20 "sure" points could be lost (or worse, end up becoming someone else's 20 points).

Lastly, this is exactly the sort of game my wife and I will play "to the penny"... which means it's wall-to-wall calculating and recalculating to try to eke out every single point. This gets tiresome and causes the last third of the game (in particular) to slow considerably for us. I suspect 90 minutes would be an absolute minimum for us, even with just two players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players are developing their own municipality in a city, attempting to score the most population points.  Straightforward playing game where players on their turn acquire a hexagonal tile from a draft/purchase line, paying the cost plus a premium based on the position in the line.  Tiles not purchased slide down the line and become cheaper.  Purchased tiles are then fitted into their suburb, triggering synergies based on adjacent tiles, other tiles in the suburb, and even other tiles in the city (i.e. the other players).  These triggers bump income (money), prestige (population growth) and instant population (victory points).  Income is earned (which may be negative), population attracted (again which may be negative, that is, they leave), and the tile drafting line adjusted.  Play until the game end is triggered, throw in some hidden bonus tiles to make it semi-interesting, most points wins.

It looks lovely, generates a nice thematic feeling of city growth.  For the first 30 minutes of this game I was quite taken with it, then it hit me why I was enjoying it so much - I was playing Through The Ages.  It went rapidly downhill from there.  As a 45 minute two player game, this would be better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
After two plays, I love how the mechanisms really compliment the theme of this game. All of the resource tracks are very intuitive, and the real-estate market seems to work smoothly. It's important not to over calculate while playing; decisions have to be made based on a gut feeling of what the best option is, rather than precise calculation of all the alternatives (otherwise AP kicks in big time); but the choices are not so overwhelming that you are left with no idea of what to do. The buying/placing decisions are definitely the fun part of this game, but once made, implementing those decisions is a bit of an accounting exercise (which is why it might work better as an iOS app where all the accounting is done for you).  

I am considering adding a house rule that player are not forced to remove tiles from the market each turn; instead they have the option. This would give a little more control over the game end condition, which I would like, but it would obviously make the game a little longer. The game is already long enough for my tastes, but it definately does not feel as long as it takes (which is a sign of a great game).

RATING: 8
After reading the rules...
Looks like a nice game that would make a good gift.

While I would like to play it sometime, it does not seem to be a game I need to own. 

UPADTE: Curse you Rhado, maybe I do need this game!

UPDATE AFTER PLAYING:
This was the first game I sought out at Unity Games 20, and I got a chance to play it with some friends.  After getting over my immediate reaction that I had played poorly, I liked it.  I really enjoyed how thematic the tiles were; the action of each made sense.  While I was punished by the impact of population growth, I appreciate why it is there.  One must prepare for the growth of their suburb or one will get bogged down towards the end.  I also did not mind the fiddliness of the scoring with each tile placement; I though that the interaction this produced was worth the hassle.

So, I would definitely like to play it again, and I think my wife would like it, but I am not quite convinced enough to buy it.  Shelf space in my house is a prized commodity, and I am not sure Suburbia makes the cut.  Suburbia gives me a similar feeling to two games that have earned shelf tenure: Attika and Vegas Showdown.  Suburbia won't be knocking either of these to the trade pile.  So, we'll see what the future brings for Suburbia and the Asher house.

UPDATE Feb 2014:  Broke down and bought it. along with expansion.  I want to play this some more to see if I can pull off managing the population growth.  This game will either open up for me, or worst case I will find it a new home.  It is officially interviewing for shelf space!

RATING: 9
I didn't expect too much when i played for the first time: during that first run i loved it!
Secret goal drives your strategy, but you can also choose to get one or more public goals.
It's interactive and got easy rules, and interesting it's the way population (=victory points) is handled: whoever has the most, wins. 
But each time a player crosses one of the red lines due to a population gain, they lose both an income and reputation point. As the little borough gets larger, it costs more to maintain. While I "hate" those red lines, as a player, I think it’s a very good choice as far as game mechanics are concerned, because it prevents early leaders from running away with the game and forces players to be careful about which tile they choose and when. 
I think it’s a nice check and balance to make sure players don’t expand too quickly, and to allow players not in first to have a chance to catch up.
Good relaxing strategy game for gamers and non-usually gamers!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Suburbia takes an odd stance somewhere between fun and mechanical, but does so with enough gusto to garner at least some level of interest from most people. The fun is borne from the same place as any tile-placement game, especially where you're given your own town to work on - watching your neighborhood grow into something magnificent.

However, the effects of these tiles leads to the biggest issue with Suburbia - constant administration. Every time a tile is put down you're required to check all of your tiles - and not just adjacent ones nor just ones put down by you. This can get particularly exhausting in the late game, and I guarantee you most players will inevitably miss something or other before the game is over.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.9
This'd be a 7 if people didn't keep suggesting it all the time.  It's a nifty game and puzzle, trying to arrange your suburb for maximum efficiency.  Lots of different buildings, although I feel like some of them are much better than others.
Money's very tight, especially later on when the prices start to shoot up.  It's nice that you always have the option to place a lake and generate some cash, but a lot of the time, they don't give you very much, and it takes your whole turn to do.  If you're not careful early, or there just aren't good income-generating choices when it's your turn, you could be forced into a spiral of insolvency.
My only serious complaint is with the PR firm and Casino buildings.  Those just seem overpowered to me.  If you get the Casino when you have a solid income base already, then you can spend the rest of the game shoveling money into reputation increases.  If you wind up with the PR firm,  you can hit 15 reputation and spend the rest of the game finding ways to fight the loss of cash.  And if you somehow get both, then you just blow the doors off the game, and everyone else might as well concede.
Still pretty much the best 'SimCity on a table' I've played.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Comments after one 4 player game.
Suburbia felt like a really great combo generating game (like if castles of Burgundy had more pasture type mechanics). You'll find yourself building your economy and attracting new residents. But at each milestone you take a hit to each track.

The fun part of the game is finding combos in the tike you have, and your opponents have. Jimmy placed a suburb, my homeowners association gives me $2, thanks Jimmy!

The downside is every move must 've reevaluated by every player to check to see if anything triggers. While I'm not too upset about this, I prefer when people pay attention to the game rather than checking out, i did find it to distract a little from the enjoyment of the game.

Compared to COMKL where that game has powers that trigger when placing tiles, and completing rooms, but no prolonged status checks.

Based on my few plays of both thus and COMKL,  this seems to be the deeper game, but I think I'd rather play castles....which before playing thus game, I would have bet it would be the other way around.

RATING: 9
Every time I play Suburbia I can't help but comment on how much of a darn fine game it is.  There's a lot to think about in Suburbia, but the turn sequence is so simple that everything gets boiled down into manageable chunks.  The tile effects cleverly match both the theme and game mechanics.  In other words, what the PR Firms, Parking Lots, Airports, Homeowner's Associations, Universities, etc. do make a whole lot of sense.   All the great and intriguing elements of city building games were carefully distilled and squeezed onto every piece of this game.  If you want a city building game, this is it!  Suburbia does lack a certain level of interaction and tension I enjoy though, but like I said, it's still a darn fine game.   I give it a lot of props as well as a lot of play.  

Two year update:  Despite it being in the top 50 games on BGG, this game doesn't get the recognition it deserves.  Perhaps it needs a graphic overhaul, more intuitive/thematic iconography, and a more effective player aid.  I could see this being a game in every family's closet.

RATING: 6
This game played exactly as I imagined it would as in good not great. Another "solid but don't need to own it" game for me (plus, my friend won it in the raffle. Huzzah!). I'm generally not a fan of tile laying games; I find them dry and abstract. But I would play this one on occasion. This should be a big hit among the BGG crowd (which values tile laying games much more than I), and the fact that I like it at all should translate as a ringing endorsement.

The bidding track appeals to me most as you jockey to be able to afford projects and you can remove projects that opponents might be gunning for. The combos force you to stay apprised of who has what tiles so you can't just sit back and play your own game in a vacuum. A solid, meaty filler but not the kind of game that will ever be a main attraction for me.

RATING: N/A
90m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Suburbia is a tile laying game. You lay different types of hex tiles to develop your suburb. Each hex tile interacts with the others in slightly different ways depending on what hexes are next to it, that you have placed anywhere and/or have been placed by the other players. The tiles can affect your population (which will also be the victory points), your income (which will decrease as your population increases) and your reputation (which will increase or decrease your population).

There are also other goals that can add to your final score if you meet the condition, some of which are open but each player also knows one secret goal. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Tile laying game where each tile interacts with all tiles next to it. Whilst this is a little fiddly at first after a round or two you get the hang of it. 

Income and reputation need to be managed and if your population grows to quickly (due to large reputation) you can suddenly find yourself very short of money as there are penalties to both income and reputation at various points on the scoreboard. This is a very clever self regulating mechanic that forces players to balance their city.

I really like that the mechanics fit the theme, for example placing suburbs next to airports has a negative effect, after all who wants to live near the airport? The stationary supply store does well when placed next to several offices, little interactions like this mean its not simply a case of purchasing the most expensive (and therefore best?) tile from the river of tiles but more about looking to purchase what is best for you at that moment... which can be totally different to everyone else at the table.

Lots of very varied strategy's here and using only half the tiles each play means a lot of re playability.

Several public goals and each player having a secret goal is also nice as it gives some focus before the game begins however some of the points awarded seem fairly random which means some goals will never be picked as they are too difficult for too little gain. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4

RATING: 5.85
I wake up in the morning and catch a ride to the factory. It’s not a great place to work, but it pays the bills. And it’s by a lake. Sometimes I ride my bike to the amusement park near the projects, or shotgun beers down by the train tracks. This used to be a small town nuzzled into the edge of the desert. Now it’s booming. But the hipsters are leaving. There’s a contiguous line of civic buildings downtown. Not a lot of business, but probably enough to win this game. 

I like the narrative arc of this game and always wanted to play sim city competitively. And I love all the different buildings and combos. But the problem is, although you are competing against your opponents on the score track, there’s very little if any interaction between the players during the game. Pretty much head-to-head solitaire with a small amount of screwage in the card row via lakes. I wish the game had more interaction and its playtime is a little too long for a game with so much luck.

Egy-két dolog miatt azonban mégsem annyira:
- a komponensek olyanok, mintha egy prototípus lenne.
- a piac elég sok szerencsét visza  játékba, mivel az állandó pénzhiány miatt reálisan, csak az első 4 lapot lehet megvenni
- a minden játékos épületére vonatkozó bónuszok észben tartása és adminisztrálása szinte lehetetlen, illetve rengeteg energiát elvesz a valódi játéktól
- végül a legkellemetlenebb. A titkos célok teljesen agyoncaspják a játékot. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
City engine building. Tiles you buy affect income and VP generation, and in most cases combine with other tiles in your or other players' suburbs for increased leverage. Buy more tiles for bigger effect. Repeat.

That might sound a bit simplistic, and it mostly is. The raw engine is easily visible through the city building theme layer, yet the game manages to stay thematic enough that the placements and their effects "make sense." The individual player goals help with diversification so that people don't go after the same tiles all the time which, along with only a selection of tiles being in play each game, hopefully dispels replayability concerns. Suburbia doesn't revolutionise urban planning, but it's a solid medium-weight design.

RATING: 1
Where to start?  Firstly I began the game making what I assumed to be a grave mistake - I poorly processed the engine at work and invested more through thematic thinking rather than game mechanics.  I made the error of purchasing cards that dealt me population (thinking I need workers) and in-advertantly purchased the VP hexes rather than the engine producing hexes.  Needless to say this drove my economy into the gutter.  I was at a penalty of minus 2 early on and with no cash on hand was forced to make lake grabs most turns, with some X2 usage to just get a small hand of cash.  After five or six further turns I had enough to purchase the most basic of hexes.  So I did. This never got me out of my death spiral financially and was bleeding money every round.  

So why am I saying all of this?  

Well I checked out of the game mentally.  I did what the game allowed me to do.  I had no real choices.  I was doomed from my early mistake.  

It must be noted I was playing with very experienced and very competent gamers.  All of whom had played before.  

So to all of our surprise when the game was over and final scoring took place I was in second over all by 2 points - beating third and fourth by 15+.  Remember I had tapped out.  I was not mentally engaged with the game, and I was resigned to make only the moves I could. No choices.  The game was running on auto-pilot for me.  

So when this occurs with competent players at the table playing to their best, and I almost win because of some balancing mechanism or randomness.   I claim broken. 

I am all for balancing designs, but when it takes meaningful play away from the game - BOOOOOOOO!!!!!

RATING: 8
I agree with Rahdo that this is one of the best (if not the best) city building games from 2013 and back.

It's wayyy better than The Capitals(which sucks) and different from City Council (which is more a political game than city building).

It also gets crazy in the mid to late game when someone puts a tile "Hey! you've put this tile in your suburb this means I have this", "Oh yeah? I also get this", "Relax guys, just look, with this tile I get ten of this, four these and three of those...". hehe! Pretty crazy game when you get to that point, especially with 3 or more players! :D

I also agree with Rahdo about the thresholds (red lines) on the population board. It's a neat trick to prevent people from hoarding "victory points" easily. You need to work for your victory points.

Although, this game is another victory point game, which I am getting tired of seeing... just too many out there, people seems to lack any form of creative ways to determine a victor to their games. Those and worker placements, I'm getting really tired of seeing those. :(

RATING: 9.5
This is currently one of my favorite games after 27 plays. This includes a combination of iOS games against various AI's, solo sessions without the bot, and 1 on 1 sessions with my gaming buddy. The solo game does not have real legs since you can adapt and exploit the same strategy to get high scores on a regular basis. Random tile selection mitigates this a bit, but if I use a strategy of building income, residential, then school and invest in school there are enough blue and green tiles to make this work solo almost every time. 

Of course, a good playing partner will identify and block your preferred strategy. This, with the addition of goals, make for an interesting play every time. I would advise to play the game solo to learn the tiles then the game really starts to shine as you plan based on the tiles coming out and the goals. I will update again after having more 2 player sessions, but I don't see this one dying out any time soon. Got the expansion which I have yet to add as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Enjoyed my first couple of plays, although it took a while to go through the rules.  It's actually a reasonable-length game, once you get used to it.

There seem to be different strategies to follow -- build airports?  Build lakes?  Build residential?  A lot could depend on the goal cards.  

Perhaps the key to this game is to play tiles in dense clusters.  If you have a concentrated tile which gives you great reputation (population income), you can double that tile with a 2x chit in the mid-game to accelerate your point production through to the finish.  Likewise, if you make a nice hole for a lake, you can get a good cash influx (and possibly double it later with another 2x chit).

It seems important to increase your income (or play lakes effectively) and save money early.  Without money, you can't buy key tiles when they come up (and a new tile comes up after every player's turn).  This is especially true when you play with more players, because you might only have one chance to buy a good tile before it's bought or trashed by someone else.

It's important to notice when a tile says "every" on it.  That means that it applies even to other tiles built by other players!  This can lead to huge windfalls, if you are aware of them... and those windfalls can go to other players just as easily, if you miss your chance.

     3) Goal - I've heard this already, but the fact that there is only 4 goals means that it is very competitive. I know that that design is a fad right now because no one likes getting 'blocked', but this negative may turn into a positive after more plays because this game doesn't think I'm a wussy and it wants me to play my best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a really great boardgame iteration of SimCity (or similar). The decisions are interesting enough both in terms of what to purchase (in light of both your immediate needs, your long term needs, and end-game goals, as well as preventing opposing players from getting that one tile they need), as well as where to place the tiles (what bonuses and penalties are applied due to this placement). Furthermore, there is the typical money-to-VP element happening here as well (i.e., develop your economy early game and make the shift towards heavy VP development mid-to-late game). However, I have seen more balanced approaches do fairly well as well. Part of this that is interesting (or potentially infuriating to those who like 'clockwork' games) is that the secret objectives might mean that everyone is 'in' until the objectives are revealed (so long as everyone plays in a way that keeps them within striking distance). I am generally neutral about this as the game is not so long that this feels too random at the end either nor is the game so long that it needs some indeterminate elements to alleviate an all-too-foreseeable ending (i.e., games where early mistakes cost you the game) but again, it injects a bit of cloudiness around what folks might be striving towards and give lagging players a hopeful outlook all the way through.

RATING: 7.75
Suburbia is a tight and well-plotted tile laying game with elements of set collection and resource management, that plays easily, quickly, and yet there is a lot going on at any one moment.

I am liking this more and more as I play it- mind you my scores aren't escalating much yet, but I am enjoying the challenge the game provides.

The speed at which this moves to conclusion is a major bonus here; it does not overstay it's welcome, and I am beginning to be tempted to just leave it out and play at least 2 games when I get it to the table.

The artwork is pleasant, 1960's era vision of the future styled, and not hard on the eyes.  The tiles are plentiful, providing lots of variation in how tile availability changes each game.  the basic actions are simple, but keeping the ship on course and moving forward can be very challenging.

I'm starting this at  7.5 after a few plays, but this may go up- especially once I snag the expansion set.

In short, this is a really solid little game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
(11/30/2013) Comment based on one play, with the Suburbia Inc. expansion.

This is an interesting take on the urban planning, mixing the feel of Carcassonne, Wealth of Nations, SimCity, and Innovation with hexes. 

Each turn a player may draft/buy a hex from a cascading row of hexes (like the cards in Through the Ages), with each hex representing a building zone, such as suburb, industry, commercial, infrastructure, and others. While there is a dollar cost for each hex, the hexes on the right have no additional cost, while the hexes on the left are progressively more expensive. You then locate the hex on your play area. Each hex has various interactions listed on it, denoting incremental/decremental income and/or influence. Income results in a cash payout each turn, allowing you to buy more expensive building hexes, while increasing influence results in population growth.

Early on, the focus is to collect hexes that generate income, and then later switch over to raise influence, in order to increase the overall population of your "suburbia". Population is essentially VP for this game. However, growing population will detract from income and influence, as it reaches specific thresholds. Re-playability is high, with each player having secret objectives, as well as a number of public objectives. Also, the type and number of hexes will vary from game to game.

Play is fast. However, given the range of choices of hexes available to the player, as well as the large decision space on where to put the hex relative to the other hexes, this is an analysis-paralysis' nightmare. With repeated plays, it is just a matter of eliminating the unresourceful combinations, and finding the optimum ones. Still, I did not really enjoy my first game, as I felt like I was playing on auto-pilot. Each turn, given the amount of money I have, there was probably one or two "best" hexes to choose from, and then locating them optimally is a matter of deduction. My first play suffered from the unfamiliarity with the various hexes and the various combos available; I simply played to survive.

In the end, the game did not click with me, since it seemed to be pretty much up to the draw, much like in Carcassonne--draw well, do well; draw badly, do badly. It also reminded me too much of Innovation, another design I dislike, trying to figure out and optimize all the interactions, but with hexes instead of cards. As for having a similar "feel" of Wealth of Nations, which is a more abstract treatment of creating a [i]nation[/i] through a network of hexes with symbols, Suburbia's abstractness came across similarly--I spent more time trying to coldly optimize/maximize/minimize hex placement, while the warm, fuzzy feeling of planning the overall aesthetics of city development (in fact, why SimCity was so much fun) went out the window. I may give it another shot, but it's very unlikely. It's not a bad game in terms of design, but it doesn't grab me. It's a "6".
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.9
1.抽任務板塊2選1，起始15元
2.買基本板塊需要付你要炸掉板塊的市場費用
3.用投資板塊也要炸掉板塊並附板塊的市場費用
4.買板塊當湖泊只需要付板塊的市場費用
5.放置投資標記需再支付一次板塊費用，但是可以在執行一次效果，並且之後能力X2
6.最後一輪板塊出現時，將當輪遊戲完成後，由起始玩家開始最後一輪，當所有人完成最後一輪後遊戲結束。
7.5元可以換人口，平手比名聲>收入>現金。

字詞解釋                                                                             
◎All other boroughs－指所有其他玩家的市鎮(不包含自己的)
◎Each adjacent－每個直接相鄰板塊的
◎Each of your－玩家擺放在市鎮裡的所有板塊(包含剛擺上去的這塊)
◎Every－每個市鎮的每個板塊
◎＄2－拿取2塊錢，包含在放置當下符合條件的板塊與之後才放置符合條件的板塊，都可以拿錢。

板塊效果執行次序                                                               
板塊放置時依下列順序執行效果：
1.支付板塊花費(加上地產市集的費用)
2.執行右上角的立即效果。
3.依據情況執行板塊下方效果
4.依據情況執行相鄰板塊效果
5.依據情況執行不相鄰板塊效果
6.查看是否有別人市鎮裡的板塊會影響你
7.查看你擺放的板塊是否會對別人的市鎮造成影響
◎如果玩家有兩張以上同樣的板塊，效果會堆疊。

RATING: 7
An interesting tile layer with multiple interactions between the tiles. The theme works well here, you can almost imagine The Sims moving into your emerging suburb. Like Agricola it gets very calculational towards the end which is a bit of a downer, and the hidden goals do introduce an element of chaos that you can't counteract. Some of the tile powers can get a bit wild (Waterfront Realty, Casino, PR Firm), I don't suppose a lot of thought went into balancing the various combos. Like most emerging designers Ted Alspach has plundered other games (Through the Ages, Age of Steam) to get his mechanisms, but this was his best game at time of publication. Castles of Mad King Ludwig is the same game with better balance.

RATING: 7
Update 10/27/15: Dropping my score after more plays. Don't get me wrong, still a very good game, but it's really dried up for me over time. I've played several more tableau/light civ builder-type games since this one, and find I like others a bit better. Would I rather play this than, say, "Among the Stars" - or, for a more direct comparison, would I rather play this than "Castles of Mad King Ludwig"? Nope.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Sim-city themed tile laying/engine-building game. Mechanically it's a hex-based Glen More minus the rondel mechanic, plus extra options, and with slightly more straightforward scoring. Each hex can affect adjacent hexes, remote hexes and some are also affected by hexes in other players' boroughs. It comes with a lot of tiles and goals, so there's lots of replayability, the setup is fairly simple, and  every game feels a bit different. The theme is excellent, and it plays silkily.

The only complaints I've got is that the art is not my style, the boards are a bit sub-par (in particular the triangle that ostensibly holds all of the games' coins), and the insert is nonexistent. I'll almost certainly invest in an organizer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
iOS App
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The rulebook is short and sweet. Definitely easy enough to set up and teach during a game night. The tiles are a nice thickness, the colors are pleasing, and there are helpful aids, both in a guide for each player, and little hints built into the board (where your income and reputation markers start, how many tiles you use for different numbers of players, etc). The start player marker is delightfully over the top, a painted wooden rectangle that gets stickers on two sides. 

One of the things I noticed in researching the game, and saw even more in playing through it, is how the designer took care to have the tile placement be thematic. You don't want a factory next to the suburbs or a park. You don't want an airport in the center of town. You want a gas station and a school next to residential areas.

I foresee lots of replayability. Only about half of the tiles, and only a few bonus tiles, are used during a game. 

There's not much in the way of direct conflict. You can take or bury a tile you think your opponent wants, and you can benefit from tiles that your opponent builds, but you can't directly destroy anything on your opponent's board.

Has a bit of a Glen More feel to it, choosing tiles and watching what's adjacent to what, but it's also about money and population and not just tile placement.

From GCL: http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/151367/item/2444837#item2444837
