RATING: 7
This is a charming game with solid thematic integration. You come out of the game with a cohesive, attractive city. I love the spatial element of planning what will go best next to each other, and I love the tiles chaining off of each other. The doubling tokens are also awesome, and clever use of those will be big. But for some reason the secret goals really rub me the wrong way. I think what bothers me most about them is that they're for majorities, so they're prone to people screwing them up for you. And this can go two ways: either they can try to suss out your goals and try to stop them, which is a mini-game I personally don't enjoy at all, or even worse your opponents just happen to buy things in a way that prevents you from scoring your goals. I haven't tried it without them but I suspect if we threw them out this would go up to 8.5 for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Lots of fun.  Totally different from my other games.  Can be a bit fiddly though.  I wish I played this more. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a game that ticks a lot of boxes, but unfortunately has some serious flaws: 

1) The game end condition is whenever the tile is drawn from the C stack, which means that the player who lucked into switching from income to vp at the right time will have an advantage.
2) The player whose private goal matches up with the public ones has a massive advantage. This can be a double edged sword though if you lose the public goal, you may lose your private goal too if someone else decides that that public goal is the one that they are definitely going to get.
3) It is possible for another player to stop you from getting your private goal accidentally if it aligns with their strategy (eg they have most greys, you have most contiguous greys, they just happen to put all their greys next to each other) or (non goal orientated: you have most lakes, another player might grab waterfront realty (before you or as well as you) and go lake mad)
4) The randomness of what tiles are available each turn, giving an advantage to whoever lucks into getting the strong income tiles early on
 
It's still a fun game now and again but can be incredibly frustrating if other players stop you from winning your goal by accident.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In this game you purchase components for your growing city (buildings, parks, lakes, etc) that provide you with income and victory points and special bonuses.  There is a strong spatial aspect to the game as bonuses often depend on the arrangement of the tiles and what they are adjacent to.  There are also combo effects.  For example, tiles are categorized and many tiles will provide benefits depending on the categories of the tiles one already has (as in bonuses for residential tiles).  This is an engrossing game that is enjoyable.  What tempers my enthusiasm is that it is a very tactical game where you are to make the best choices each turn but while there are paths to victory there isn't a lot of strategic planning or interesting combos.  I leave the game feeling satisfied but not hungry to play again or with ideas I'd like to try out in a future game.

RATING: 7
Suburbia is an enjoyable take on city-building. It has some issues in that there is quite a bit of bookkeeping, certain hidden goals can make the game harder for certain players, and that, with more players, the tiles can come out in an order that makes them near impossible for certain players to have a chance at obtaining them. In spite of those flaws, it still is quite a bit of fun if for no other reason that the feeling of building one's city and seeing it's economy, reputation, and population grow.

RATING: 5

Initial Rating: 5.0 (August 2013)

I expected more from this game. It is gorgeous, the components are very well done and it has a lot of replayability, however the decisions involved in the game are rather obvious, so luck plays a huge role deciding if you get the right buildings at the right time. The first 30 minutes were pretty fun, however the game is too long for what it offers.

I´m not sure how I feel about the catching mechanism (red poles) in the score track, it seems it punish good players and allows a very similar score, still I have only played once so I´m, not sure this is the case. Also I´m worried about the Casino and PR tiles, they are (more or less) exempt from the balancing mechanism, making them overpowered.

The game is easy to learn and teach, but it is too light for my taste, I will give it another chance in the future to properly decide if I like it or not. For now it is out of my “to buy” list.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Suburbia is enjoyable for me despite being far lighter than my usual tastes.  Part of this is due to the city-building theme.  I've been a fan of city sims ever since SimCity 2000 for the PC.  I even like Urban Sprawl a great deal, despite the Chad Jensen title having been rejected by many for its computational complexity and chaos.

Suburbia itself is mechanically a simple tile-drafting game where the complexity comes from the tile interactions and the end-game goals.  I worry about players getting frozen out of income generators through little fault of their own though.  There's a significant snowball effect and getting left in the dust has proved fatal in my experience and those of my friends.

I can see wanting to bring this game out in a lot of 3-4 player situations where we have an hour.  Not sure I want to devote more than 10 plays to it, though.

RATING: 8.5
- I wish they had chosen a better colour scheme for this game and made better artwork. Had FFG got hold of this, but kept all the mechanics the same, I feel it would be way more popular.
- I really enjoy the mechanics, but some of the buildings/tiles are pretty boring and drab.
- The way your per-turn income and scoring decreases as you reach scoring milestones is brilliant. This presents an interesting speed/timing decision. You generally want to build an income engine early game, and transition into a points engine mid-to-late game. The question is when to transition, and can you maintain enough income to ensure you don't stall when you jump up in points.
- The personal and public goals are also great. They give players just enough incentive to compete for things without massively punishing the players that choose to abstain. It's rare a player will be able to complete multiple public goals and their own private goal as these often go against each other. 
- I also really like the lake mechanism. We all take pleasuring in laking a tile another player clearly wants.
- Plays well with all player counts, teaching is pretty easy.
- Recommend the first expansion. The border tiles are a fun edition as they provide extra choices.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

This has a surprisingly decent solo game option, which I suspect enhances its appeal with the gamer crowd. In this game you simply place tiles which have their own effects as well as effects that apply depending on whether other tiles have been played and/or are adjacent. Not terribly exciting, but easy to explain and just engaging enough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
From the Spiel 2012 flood of city building games this seems to be the one coming closest to the ideal of a Sim City boardgame. In principle, it's a simple tile-laying game with no really original elements but the combination of economic management balanced with population growth works fine. Different goals and using only a subset of tiles each game guarantee replayability although I doubt that there are alternatives to the general strategy of first building up income and timing the switch from income generation to population growth correctly. A slight drawback is the fiddlyness of executing all effects from tile placement which can be too confusing for occasional gamers. Once these effects are internalized, it plays smoothly and is one of the few games that should finish in less time than the 90 minutes stated on the box. I'm interested in getting a copy which is high praise from me for a game involving combo building.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Well placed themed in a very euro-lite game. The rules aren't complicated, but playing it can be a bit difficult. But the complicated nature of playing is also what makes this game so good. You need to pay attention to what everyone is building in their 'burbs. Because what someone else builds may increase your income, or change your reputation (Which causes people to move in or out of your city). You'll be placing buildings to try and balance income, and control your population growth.

The next interesting part of the game is the scoring/population track. The winner of the game essentially is the one with the largest population, but as you grow in population, the cost to run it increases and desirability of your city decreases. So you have to be set and prepared to gain points.

RATING: 10
The city-building game that finally Got It Right.  Suburbia provides an excellent city building experience with well designed thematic connections - no one needs to ask why people don't want to move to the borough where there are landfills in everyone's back yard.  One of the brilliant decisions in the production of Suburbia is the plentiful oversupply of tiles - in most games, only about half the available tiles are in play, increasing variability and making you less sure of how you're going to proceed toward your goals.  This is the first game that's given me "SimCity in a box" - a definite 10.  Now maybe Ted Alspach can give me the occasional earthquake or monster attack :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Oh, how I love this game! You have the joy of creation as you watch your corner of the city grow and evolve as you carefully shepherd it towards one or more of the end goals. I like the fact that there are limited ways to truly cripple your opponents. I'm rather bad at it and rarely win, yet still enjoy playing it every time. Also, the fun doesn't stop when the final scores are revealed- you then have the entertainment of looking at each other's towns and figuring out exactly where they have built. This is even better with the expansion.
The one niggle I have is the goals- a lot of them are "most X" or "least X" goals. If you're unfortunate enough to get a personal goal that is in direct conflict with one of the public goals then you can end up significantly disadvantaged. In addition, the "least" goals can really limit your options and lead to a rather crippled city. We tend to house-rule, removing 50% of the "least" goals before shuffling and drawing private and public goals, which really helps this problem. Other very minor niggle is that some of the cardboard components tore during punching, leaving them identifiable from the back. All this aside, an excellent game.

RATING: 8
I strongly prefer games where you are playing other players, rather than multi-player solitaire. There are exceptions and this is one of them. One of the best. 

What I like most is how smooth the game plays and the interesting combinations you can develop. Every game I play results in a vastly different city. In this sense the game does involve a lot of indirect player interaction because you need to respond to what other players are taking from the real estate market and adjust based on that. You can't just follow your own plan, ignore what other players are doing, and expect to do well. However, the game still has a strong multi-player solitaire feel about it and that's my only negative about the game. It's fast, fun, clever, and by the end, satisfying.

RATING: 8
A fun game that I always enjoy playing and introducing to others.  The goals provide you with some long term planning potential, while reacting to the changing real estate market and your opponents' moves is primarily tactical.  I like how the complexity is emergent from the tile interactions rather than a heavy rule set.  A lot of replayability in the box from the different goals, and the fact that not every tile is used in each game.  I also appreciate the modular board/setup that can easily accommodate different table sizes and player counts.

Some people may not like that there is no way to track different tile interactions (other than mentally), so for example you may forget you get a certain bonus when someone plays a type of tile, but I have not found this to be too much of an issue, it just necessitates paying attention to other players' moves.  Also, a small minority of the tiles might be considered duds that are hardly ever bought (I have yet to play a game where somebody purchased the new car dealership), but this is a minor quibble.

All in all a great design, and one of my favorite middle weight euros.  

approximate number of plays (pre-2017):  15

RATING: 2
The first time I played this it was alright. I wasn't jumping for joy with each move, but all in all, not bad.

The second time I played this, it was terrible. The tiles in the starting market were all WAY too expensive for the amount of starting money we got. There was only one affordable one, and since my opponent was start player, it was gone before I ever got a turn. From the starting move on, I was unable to afford anything that generated recurring funds. I had to turn buildings into lakes the whole game, and then immediately get the meager amount of money that this made for me taxed away for the sin of progressing on the population track.

I'm okay with losing a game. But to have fun, I need to have the feeling that I can use my ideas to make some kind of self-determinative progress. The rules for Suburbia seem to be written so that if you have a poor hand (or a poor place in line at an ill-stocked market) from the beginning, then you're basically screwed. You now have to sit there for the next hour going through the motions so that the other players can take their turns. That doesn't seem like a well-thought-out design. Even if someone could guarantee that this would never happen to me again, I just don't want to play a game where it can happen to ANYONE. Games should be for having fun—for ALL the players to enjoy themselves trying different tacks even if an initial poor decision or poor hand means they won't actually win the game.

Suburbia just doesn't fit the bill.

If you want to build futuristic cities, play [thing=128271][/thing]. Heck, even [thing=25568][/thing] is a better city-building game better than this, and it's an abstract. You want markets? Play [thing=2651][/thing]. Don't play Suburbia.

RATING: N/A
Nathri 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is tough, on the one hand it works really well, lots of depth, and tons of enjoyment to be had.  On the other, it sometimes feels like work, and also the production quality has left something to be desired.

Starting with the good.  The game's design if very good.  I don't own another tile laying game, and this one has a lot of very good mechanics behind it.  We play power grid, and the new tiles follow sort of a "market" value for new tiles.  Unlike most games, the highest spot actually has a chance of being purchased.  You can strategically gain a lot by buying an overpriced tile.  This is all new to me and a welcomed change.

The other nice thing is the fact that you have indirect influence over the other players from their tiles, your tiles, or goals.  Manipulating the market to force others to spend a ton of money, or taking less than optimal tiles are some examples.  Leaving high cost tiles in the lowest market value blockading low income players from making big moves are just great.  Dumping off certain tiles to force others to take a certain type of tile (Industry / Commercial / Residential) so they'll lose the goals for points at the end game are so nicely done.

All the while you're going to have to watch what you're building because a wrong tile can give benefit to your opponent instead of yourself.  Or you might have the right tile but no where to put it.  You have to watch the market to make sure a tile you want stays on the board by the next turn, and also keep your balance up so you can build up your own combos.

The game is pretty solid as far as risk / reward goes too.  Is it better to put your eggs in one basket and go with an airport route early?  The tiles early on provide very little bonus, but as the game goes on, others can benefit off your early plans.  However, if you can secure all of them and multiple tiles comes out per phase, you're in a great spot to win the game.

My wife actually played this game and enjoyed it thoroughly.  I have yet to play with more groups, but she's asking for more.  I've played a single player game, and three, three player games.  I think from the positives alone, I can give this game a solid 8.  I like it a bit more because it seems to draw itself out for more plays.  Popularity helps with the score.

Now, not everything is sunshine and rainbows.  When I first saw the reviews of this game, I knew calculating the scores can be daunting.  It can actually go both ways with that.  I first tried the solo game and it was a lot to take in at once.  Despite only having a single city to concentrate on, it had a lot of back and fourth with the scoring.  You had to calculate what is happening, what has happened, all while learning the tiles.  My first play was a total bust and I had to restart mid way.  My 2nd game ended with more understanding but it took nearly 2 hours for me to go through it going back to the rules as I played.  I would strongly suggest playing multiplayer first before tackling a solo game.

Now, the next three plays I played was three players.  As each player keeps an eye out on their own tiles, scoring is a LOT easier.  Less planning ahead because you don't have the extra step in solo rules, and you can plan while you wait.  Actually that's one positive I left out.  Productive downtime is a very common thing in this game.  Still, as the game played on, and by time our third game drawn out, people got lazy, attention spans start to grow short and I had to now keep an eye out on three sets of tiles for everyone.  Forgetting to score things started to creep up, and it became a small bit of chore to continue the game.

Now, if you're playing with people who are all into the game, I don't think this will be a problem.  So I took a point off purely for the fact that you can hit or miss with the scoring.  I know with more plays it will get easier, but I see it as a problem in the future after a bit of time off from the game that relearning the tiles will be a bit tricky again.

Lastly, I'm not exactly thrilled with the many comments about the quality of the production.  My copy came with a couple of tear edges on tiles.  The tile cut out was of very bad quality.  It's definitely the worst production of all the games I've owned (only after my King of Tokyo copy), I have yet to follow up with the company to receive replacements on my tiles, but the saving grace is, it is still a playable game with the damages.  Unfortunately, I keep my items in pristine condition and to me, this poor quality is unacceptable.  I won't take points off for this however, because I focus more on game play experience.  Otherwise I think the poor quality would drive this otherwise great game into maybe a 5 - 7 rating.

Still, I think I'm glad to have this in my collection, and will be playing this many more times in the years to come.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

For a game with simple turns there are many choices to make. Only issue, remembering to score everything when it may affect other players and adjacent tiles is hard. People with constantly need to adjust stuff as they missed one of the tiles.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8.5
This is an outstanding board game. I bought this thinking it would be a nice little game for two people, but I was very, very wrong. This is a great strategy/(IMO)casual game to play on any weeknight. It is competitive, but not too competitive to put you on edge. The entire game I feel like I'm going to lose, right up until the secret and public objectives get counted for. Up until this happens, depending on the strategy I take, I may be pleading with the game for that last round tile to come out or vice versa. This game is designed wonderfully. The only downside to this game that I see is the sheer amount of information you're managing while playing it. (However, this could be looked at as a feature of the game) Sometimes it gets a little overwhelming with just three or four players when you have to do all of the checks for a tile you place down(this can even lead to forgetting to do something). Nonetheless, to me, Suburbia is a must own board game and is a great casual/strategic game.

[size=12][b]Top 100-200 board games list[/b][/size]

[size=12][b]Why I like Suburbia:[/b][/size]
+ The various tile interactions are fairly balanced. 
+ I like the mechanics
+ I like the theme
+ The game is easy to teach
+ The duration of the game is fine
+ There seems to be high replayability with the Suburbia Inc expansion (that I have yet to play with)
+ The game requires meaningful decisions to win
+ There is the right amount of decisions

[size=12][b]Fixable dislikes in regards to Suburbia:[/b][/size]
/ The game is missing an extension to the board. You can find this file on BGG.
/ There are a lot of things to keep track of throughout the game. Some players for some reason fail to read the player aid and I find myself repeating the process too many times or doing the process for the player. I suggest playing with people who are willing to read the player aid. 
/ Money seems to be a non-issue. I wish money was tighter so that player order is not as much of an issue in regards to buying the strong tiles that come out at the end of the cue. I think that a variant that increases the cost of the final spot would fix this issue but I have yet to implement this.

RATING: 7.7
This is the board game version of Sim City. The game mechanism faithfully reflects the city building theme. Although the tiles look boring, most of them do make thematic sense. I also like that there are common goals AND a personal goal, secretly chosen out of the two given at the beginning. Those goals offer a fair amount of variety. Otherwise, every game would be quite similarly scripted. What I mildly dislike is that one has to keep checking all the previously played tiles, including other players', since all of them affect and are affected by the new tile. I know it is an essential aspect of the game, but it requires unusual scrupulousness.

RATING: N/A
Suburbia is a tile-laying game in which each player tries to build up an economic engine and infrastructure that will be initially self-sufficient, and eventually become both profitable and encourage population growth. As your town grows, you'll modify both your income and your reputation. As your income increases, you'll have more cash on hand to purchase better and more valuable buildings, such as an international airport or a high rise office building. As your reputation increases, you'll gain more and more population (and the winner at the end of the game is the player with the largest population).

During each game, players compete for several unique goals that offer an additional population boost – and the buildings available in each game vary, so you'll never play the same game twice!

RATING: 8

The playability & relatively quick play makes this worthwhile.

Eines meiner Lieblingsspiele... was soll ich sagen? :)
Wenn man das Spiel kennt, hat man schon Vorteile gegenüber einem, der die Plättchen nicht kennt und nicht weiss was da noch kommen kann.
Wer an der Reihe ist, darf sich von den ausliegenden Plättchen eines aussuchen und in seine Vorortsiedliung einbauen. Das kostet Geld und bringt Einkommen oder Ruhm. Je nachdem wo das Plättchen hingelegt wird gibt es Synergie-Effekte. Wohn und Gewerbegebiete neben einen Park bringt Ruhm, was wiederrum Einwohner beschert (Siegpunkte). Industrie zerstört zwar mein Ansehen, vor allem wenn man sie neben die Wohnsiedliung baut, aber erhöht mein Einkommen, was mir Geld bringt um neue Plättchen kaufen zu können. Dieser Sinn dahinter, dass sich die Plättchen sinnhaft gegenseitig beeinflussen finde ich an dem Spiel so toll.
Ich spiele es immer wieder gerne ;)

RATING: 7
Another city-building game, this one didn't skimp on the theme (though we did end up building a city in which one could go to elementary school and college, but all the high schools were at the bottom of lakes). The game isn't bad, but very mathy. Every tile placement requires you to see what that tile scores. Then see if it changes the score of any tiles next to it. Then see if it changes any of your other tiles. Then see if it changes anyone else's tiles. Then go to suburbia.com to see if it changes the tiles in any other games of Suburbia currently being played in the world. Okay, that last one is a lie, but still there's a lot of calculation and recalculation. None of it is difficult, but it does increase the chances of discovering at some point that you shorted yourself some income or something. This one's supposedly coming out on the iPad soon and I think it's probably better suited for that.

In addition, the goals you're trying to achieve are hugely swingy and it's possibly to be dealt a personal goal that isn't worth much even if you do achieve it. But that's something that repeated plays might reveal to be not much of a problem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Hugely enjoyable.  Once the rhythm of the game is understood it moves smoothly but has enough decisions to make you think and keep your attention throughout (our game played between 75-90 minutes but I am confident we could hit the 1 hour mark with more play).  The choosing of the tiles means you are primarily playing against to the left and right of you however the common goals and tiles which trigger scoring opportunities across the table mean everyone around the table is involved and attention should be paid to all choices.

The designer's connections to Age of Steam is evident at a surface level with some scoreboard features and tiles.  However, more importantly the similarity is apparent in how strongly the mechanics support the theme and the need for a 360 view as each aspect has an impact on the other. Entirely engrossing.

A refreshing contrast to a plethora of kickstarter dice fuelled games I have faced recently. A welcome change.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
While my initial fears that it is really easy to forget of effects that proc off nonadjacent tiles, this game has also fulfilled my expectations of a game that combines simplicity with a high number of choices and good theme implementation (despite the only okay art scheme). Furthermore, this game is very easy to learn (only 3 pages of rules if you exclude the solo variant) and seems to incentivize different play styles depending on which goals are available. 

Knowing what to maximize your limited money in the early game and when to switch from boosting income to boosting reputation in the midgame is key, and if you can pull ahead casino and PR firm seem very strong (but doing so too soon will leave you floundering late game). 

This game has no direct interaction, but you can definitely deny people some key tiles. Because of this, I think this game plays best with 3, as with 4 it is much harder to pull off strong synergies (however it still plays well with 4), and 2 does not offer as much competition for goals.

RATING: 8
It's SimCity in board form!

The big swing point to be aware of is the presence of the Casino and PR Firm buildings in Phase 3. They're the ones that let you ignore the diminishing returns of income and reputation as your population rises, and can easily be the most powerful buildings in the game. If some players aren't aware of them, and aren't thinking about holding enough cash to grab them at first opportunity, the knowledge gap can effectively determine the game with a missed opportunity to buy them.

RATING: 6
Suburbia is a solid attempts at a city building game.  The game has you buy hex tiles and you place them with other tiles.  Certain tiles will have benefits if placed next to other tiles.  Also, it can have benefits if other players use certain types of tiles.  You have secret objectives in the game and public objectives.  These objectives can represent a very large percentage of overall game scoring can be very "swingy".  The game suffers from having to constantly remember that certain tiles have benefits when other tiles, including opponent tiles are placed.  Even with all of this, it is still an pretty fair game.

RATING: 6
I love that someone has finally made what amounts to a board game adaptation of SimCity. Unfortunately, I wasn't as smitten with this as I hoped I'd be. It doesn't help that I find Ted Alspach's art style off-putting, but the real problem for me was that the game seemed to inconsistently alternate between tiles that have a once-off effect, tiles that do something based on the neighbouring tiles, and tiles that use the whole game state. The latter were problematic in that at times it seemed I needed to keep track of the game state at all times. Computer games can do this, but board games shouldn't.

I'd be happy to give this game a few more goes if it was suggested, but I can't see myself adding it to my collection.

RATING: 5
I’m in a minority when it comes to this game, obviously, as most folks seem to love it. To a certain extent, I can understand their stance, since building up your little city in an optimal way, making the best use of the tiles on offer, is certainly fun. And while the various tile interactions are possibly more involved than they have to be, I would gladly swallow the extra book-keeping, since you get back both an enlarged decision space and a small taste of the complexity that a proper city represents.

Regretfully, Suburbia throws all this into the bin with the lazy (and frankly, a bit baffling) design choice of using all-or-nothing goals in what purports to be a mid length strategy game. That some of these goals are hidden, giving you the opportunity to screw over another player accidentally, just adds insult to injury. For me, this turns a promising and well-developed game structure into an overlong luckfest.

I’m prepared to make an exception for the two-player game, because a lot of what irks me about the arbitrary nature of the goals will be less of a problem there – at least you clearly know who to target with counterplans. With more players, however, this is not something I’m keen on playing again.

RATING: 9
What a smartly designed game. So simple: buy a tile, lay a tile, collect income.
I am normally not fond on tile-laying games, because there is not much strategy in them. But this game is different in that perspective.
There are initial goals - hidden and open, for which players are competing. Furthermore, all tiles interact, on different levels and with different aspects, which introduces new (sub)goals.
The consequence is that each player desires almost every tile, but for a different reason. There are many paths to victory, but all go through the same set of ever contested tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Suburbia is a game great in mechanics and filled with theme. Each turn is simple, with the player playing 1 out of 2 possible actions. However, the repercussions affect the player until the end of the game (like throwing a rock into a lake and seeing the ripples form, except the ripples become bigger as time passes). This creates different strategies for different gamers. Never once did I feel I could not do anything on my turn. There's always something good out of everything you do. The difference between a good and a great players is the ability to plan ahead. Still, there are goals that can balance the game so that losing players always have a chance to win. This, for me, is a great game that's easy to learn, but hard to win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Less convniced with 5* and certainly wouldnt recomend as a 5 player game as feel would go to long.

RATING: 7.5
Suburbia is a tough nut to crack. I definitely enjoyed it, but we had trouble getting going. The directions, in my opinion, are not well articulated and not comprehensive. As a result, some of the placement rules are unclear at first, particularly tiles with ongoing effects. Our first game was a write off. At the beginning of the second game, we were still checking the rules (in vain) for clarification. Don't get me wrong. The game is not that complicated. But there are some important distinctions that have to be made between their effects. So, the rules get a C-. The game itself though is fun and quite different. It is a thinky game, not an "exciting" game necessarily, but there is a unique satisfaction to building your own city. I like it, and I hope that I grow to like it more with future plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game simulates planning and building rival cities and competing for various resources.  Each turn a player adds one tile to their growing city, and then adjusts their population and money accordingly.

The designers here guaranteed a different experience every time by including more tiles than needed (so you only play with a subset), and introducing various public and private goals to compete for throughout the game.  One game I double-invested in Casinos, while another I never saw a single one.  This little bit of unknown information really goes a long way here: are Offices in short supply this time?  Are we all avoiding Industrial Districts like the plague?  Tune in next time to find out!

From the actual playing stand point, the turns are short and simple, with only two resources to really track (income and population).  I was not expecting to like such a simple design, but I love it.  Rather than focus exclusively on getting those two numbers very high, you're usually focused on combining adjacency bonuses on your city tiles.  Two players may have rich towns but accomplish that in completely different ways.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.99
The solo variants are essentially there to help teach you the game. The solo variant without the bot has zero tension and it is merely you trying to score as many points as possible without any boost from another player. The bot is very easy to beat. They generate a ton of money so that's the only thing you really need to worry about. You can intentionally screw over the bot.

Multiplayer is really the only way to play this game. I have yet to do four player though even though it is suppose to play best at that number. Four players will make the amount of turnover for the tiles to be on the high side so it could make it harder for players to plan out better. More players means more ties for goals. This in a way can free up strategy as you might not get the chance to win certain goal rewards anyway.

There's extra stuff to keep track of due to all of the bonuses with the interconnecting suburban areas. That aspect might be the thing that takes to down a notch from being a gateway, but I have had little issue with it. The rulebook is four pages, and the theme is so well done it is easy for people to pick up outside of tracking bonuses

Explaining a couple of the the goal tiles in the rulebook would have been nice. The fewest/lowest goals can be annoying for some and can honestly make the game more difficult to win even with higher rewards. Some combos are more difficult to pull off than others, IE office titles, but some of them are done for thematic reasons.

Currently my #3 game. rating wise it is tied with others but wins out due to plays and total time invested.
 
Rating Potential: 8-9. Someday I'll try to figure out if it deserves the full 9+ or not. It would make it close enough to a 10 to where I would wonder why it isn't. I'm just not sure I'm ready to put it on the level of Mage Knight and Summoner Wars. Hearing the expansion was built along with the base game confirms that this wasn't as much of a full blown game that it could have been, and if everything was there from the start, it would be an instant 10. It is still "kind of" awesome though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An excellent game.  My son also enjoys it, which says a lot as he generally does not like most board games.

Fairly easy rules (although sometimes there is a lot to keep up with when it comes to scoring and tile bonuses).  The game has a great graphic design and is aesthetically pleasing.  At the end of the game I enjoy looking at the city I have built.

Theme is the most important aspect for me in a game, and I was originally unsure how I'd feel about Suburbia.  I want to make sure when I build my city everything fits and makes sense; not just putting tiles down for the sake of getting points.  I am happy to say that Suburbia manages the theme quite well.  You don't want factories next to residential areas, you get more people when you have schools, and you make people happy by building parks (which cost money to upkeep),etc.  

There isn't a whole lot of player interaction, but there can be, depending how you want to play.  I was a big fan of SimCity growing up, and this gives me that feeling again.  Out of all the city building board games out there that I've seen, Suburbia looks the best, to me at least; aesthetics are important in a city building game, such as this, as you want your city to look like a city.

RATING: 7

RATING: 3
Yawn... Now this game is fine, perfectly fine, slick modern clear graphics and clean intuitive gameplay (for the most part).

However, it's just an exercise in snowballing and growth, so much growth. As such, it is only worthwhile to play alone against AIs on your tablet - when you have nothing else to do... I will say that the "gates" that have you lose income makes for a nice challenge, and setting up your city to get the most advantage when you change gears from income to pop / rep is a tricky puzzle. 

Also, the triggers that has you looking over at your opponents cities are more clunky than rewarding from an interaction standpoint and I almost wish they just cut it altogether so that the only screwage would be "Damn! you took my tile!"

Playable, amusing enough, but just a bit meh...

RATING: 7.4
Nice theme and artwork. Got that Sim City feeling, including the lack of player interaction. Sure, some buildings is affected by other players cities and you do compete for the same tiles on the track, but the main focus is always your own city. That, combined with the cute art style makes a round of Suburbia a cheerful event.

RATING: 9.4
This is my favourite euro so far with possible contention from Shipyard should I ever get a copy / finish a full game. It's easy to teach and simple to play. Scoring is a little tricky for new players, but some small transparent discs should solve that problem. 

Every game plays out differently depending on what tiles are available and which public / private goals come out. Each player count has a different feel, but all play equally well. Lower player counts are more strategic but higher player counts are more interactive. Best of all is the way suburbs grow and stories evolve organically. Each player evolves their own identity and town history, sometimes to hilarious effect, which is a rare quality in a euro.

RATING: 6.5
I rather suspect the one game I have played so far of Suburbia was atypical in that all open goals were to be the worst at something. This, and my hidden goal to have the most green tiles, pretty much drove my entire strategy. I was terrible for everything except worker housing and my Soviet-style town managed to cash in big on bonuses having the least amount of imperialist capital blue developments (no need for a convenience store when the state supplies all your needs), worst reputation (jealousy from Capitalist Roaders who envy life in the Workers' Paradise no doubt) and the worst performing economy (down to kulak/Trotskyite wreckers). 

The game itself was quite enjoyable and played well, but as I was attempting to be the worst at everything I didn’t really get a ‘fair’ view of how the game plays. I did, however, win by a big ol’ heap of points, a fine victory for the people and thorough vindication for applied scientific socialism.

I would try this game again just to see if the victory conditions can be less extreme, i.e. economic and social success is rewarded. At this stage Suburbia is not on the ‘must purchase’ list but this may change after another game or three.

RATING: 6
[b]Like:[/b]
-Pretty simple, as long as you can calculate a bit
-Discovering big combos is very satisfying, especially the investment marker (this lessens with playthroughs, as you're pulling off the same combos again and again)
[b]Dislike:[/b]
-No interaction (except buying stuff your opponent might want)
-Fiddly calculations, especially for new players
-More people makes the game worse

So many interesting decisions the make, and the effects of every tile fit the theme enjoyably perfect. The investment marker is one of the most satisfying pieces/mechanics I've come across in gaming. Initially, the game can also pretty funny, but that fades away after a few games.

I find it a little too bad though that it can get a little fiddly when a lot of effects are effecting each other, but finding that awesome combo that raises a certain stat sky-high is just awesome! Also, I hate how little interactivity there is between the players. It has that thing I don't like in gaming where in the end you're just like "ow, seems like I played a little more efficient than you." If a game doesn't benefit from more players, it's not something I'm really excited about. You don't really care what your opponents are building in this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
An interesting tile-laying game, quite similar to Glen More in that adding new tiles would trigger the other tiles in your tableau in different ways, so what to acquire and how to place the tile is a crucial part of the game.

The goal seems to be the central focus of the game - probably more so than the tiles, as they add a lot to the final score. Often the tiles are balanced enough that any approach you make is decent as long as you make sense, so everyone' score will be similar. 

The theme is here, the game play is satisfying, however I wonder if the game necessarily rewards good play, especially with the bonus tiles. Often these will affect the final scoring much more than how well you manage the city throughout the game.

The game has a very good core. It just needs a lot of fine-tune to be a good game.

RATING: 8

RATING: 7
Played 4p and really liked it (though there were some nitpicks). I expect 3p would be better, but I love the theme and logical consistency, the clean yet systematic styles, and how you can combo based on how you build the suburb. Spacewise, its not too big, and it feels thematic with the actions.

I was not crazy about watching my tiles for each little activation, but I suspect getting some tokens to mark which tiles activate things would help (and also some are only for adjacency). I also kind of wish the artwork was more vibrant, but I understand thats the cost for its small, portable size. I also didnt like some goals tiles and think it couldve been clarified a bit more. And some, like "least income" or "least reputation" (both of which I had had) felt counterintuitive to do while scoring points.

I like how building your money first is important since the red bars keep the winners down, that is a good design imo. I was also a bit biased since I won with 125 (and couldve won with 140) so I feel good about that game. Another minor nitpick is the luck in the tile flip, timing, and individual bonus markers, but I think this is necessary to give it a lot of replayability and force the players to think and mitigate. The individual bonus markers also make it a mystery who is in the lead and the red bars keep the bonus tiles relevant, preventing runaway leaders too much from what I can see.

Overall, I really loved it. It was a lot more charming than I had expected. I like this more than CoMKL too, since its so much smoother and cleaner. I also really love how the points is called Population, and the naming of income and reputation. Brilliant little thematic touch!

Plays: 4+

After a couple plays, I find myself finding this game just a bit on the light side. I wish there was more gameplay or depth and its felt a bit more fiddly watching for activations than I like.

Reasons for trading away: too light, a tiny bit fiddly to set up (expansion actually made this worse), 2p mode sucks (or is subpar at least), isn't actually that newb friendly with all the constant checks and updates to your board (I don't mind but its a little annoying when I have to keep checking for others or when I dont feel as much of a challenge), and its a rather simple strategy game (just play conservatively on your 0, +2 tiles and keep income up in beginning, switch to reputation midway, get your goal tiles and at least one center tile, and you're set). I didn't find it mindblowing in anyway though the theme helped but just wasn't an amazing game that made me excited like PG or HT were and not as interesting long term after the initial luster wore off. There are also pretty much zero clever plays or memorizing moments that I can recall - its just a solid game with a solid experience and very little wrong but not a whole lot right.

RATING: 8
There are lots of options in this game, but it is quite tough to figure out.  There is a run away leader rule that keeps people from getting too far behind, but a pro at this game will destroy you.

It can be frustrating learning because there are populations and income to concern yourself with and as you move up in money, population goes down.  It becomes a kind of optimal brain-teaser that can get tough.

The way the different tiles work together gives you ample amount of flexibility to build the city that you are keen on...as close to Sim City as I have seen in a board game!

RATING: 7.5

-----
Upped from 6.5. The Glen More likeness is more the mnemonic than the actual mechanic, but either way it's burdensome, and there are probably a few tile effects that get overlooked in each game -- really, you need a computer for this type of chore work. Great gateway game if you can get past the hurdle of explaining how the tile effect mechanic works. The high novelty factor wears off after a few games once the tiles have all been seen.
-----
Realize now that it's meant to be much lighter and disposable than all that. Suburbia as Cribbage. Luck of the draw plays an outsize role, but the game is short enough that it feels right.

RATING: 7
One of the easiest games to explain to non-gamers I've seen in ages: Folks, it's Sim City: The Board Game, and it fits that definition to a T.  The only reason I'm not rating it higher is a) the fiddliness of the pieces is really off-putting to non-gamers, which negates the initial appeal, but if they can push through there's enjoyment to be had; and b) there seems to be a clear path to victory from the tiles in any given game... and the game doesn't announce that path until the 3rd Act, where you've either developed the right path for the C tiles or you're not as lucky.

Also, while goals create extra tension and strategy, hidden goals kind of break the game for me. Drops to 5.5 with hidden goals.

Still, a rather fun game and my favorite from Ted.

RATING: 8.5
I really wanted to like this game - and fortunately I did!

It's a tableau builder with some nice ideas. A spatial element is always interesting, and the ability to key off different subsets of tiles (including your opponents) allows for a variety of different effects. As with other engine builders, knowing the available tiles helps your cause greatly.

The only criticisms I have are that games can sometimes be samey (get income with blue/yellows, get reputation with gray/green), and in person it is extremely difficult to keep track of all the effects.

RATING: 7.7
What an awesomely balanced and fun city-builder, probably the best one out there.  The game plays very intuitively and everyone becomes very invested in their city.  After the first 1 or 2 games you will really love your creation.

This is one of those games where even when you lose it doesn't really bother you because you made an entire city with its own unique personality.

So is the game interactive with other players?  Definitely.  Some parts of your city can affect others and vice versa.  You always want to know how you're benefiting or hurting another player as it can mean victory or defeat.  The scores usually stay pretty even towards the end of the game so every move counts!

Even solo play is fun!

RATING: 9.5

RATING: 7
I think it might be best with 2 or 3, with attention paid to blocking and disrupting the competitors.

The premise is simple- grow a small town into a large town, buying developments and adding them to your individual array. Some developments only effect adjacent tiles, others your whole town, and some effect every player. These are not once-off events, either: effects have continuous duration so upkeep can become complex, albeit not onerous.

I quite like Suburbia. It isn't a brain burner (hello City Tycoon) and plays very quickly, with more interaction than I was expecting. There is plenty of opportunity to annoy other players by way of interfering with the market of available tiles.

[b]Suburbia[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Plays well with 2, I also really enjoy it with 3 (haven't played with 4 and I'm not sure I want to).
- Sim City the board game, enough said.
- Nice components, I actually really like the graphic design and aesthetics in this game. Once you finish constructing your city it looks great.
- I really enjoy all the different ways the various tiles interact with each other, and the variety of the buildings themselves.
- Having to balance your income versus reputation is really difficult and since different tiles come out every game, this is a game you can play a lot.
- This game is usually pretty tight and the winner is not decided till the end.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]We have played 3p and mostly 2p games so far and we all love this game. It absolutely recreates the feeling of playing SimCity when I was a kid.

I love the graphic design and the simple aesthetics of the game, once your city is built it is beautiful. We always spend time after playing to describe what kind of city we built and if it reminds us of a city we have been to/ or know about. Excellent game, this is the replacement for Monopoly my son has been looking for since his favorite aspect of that game was buying properties and making $. I can't recommend this game highly enough!

As far as playing with my family, I believe my 8 year old daughter could handle it, but so far she has not wanted to play this one yet. My son (11) is excellent at the game and has no problems keeping track of all the information in the game on each turn.[/i]

RATING: N/A
A simple and clever game of building a city. You and your fellow players each build their own section of the city. But all players can benefit or suffer from buildings built in all players' sections of the city. For example if you build a building that gives you +1 income for all restaurants, you would score +1 for every restaurant built so far by all players in the game. 

The game turn is as simple as purchasing a tile and placing it in your city area. Then you collect the income and reputation based on how the tiles interact with your other tiles and other players' tiles. 
Wonderful game, great for 2 players, probably even more fun with more and a great week night game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I very much enjoy this game.  The hardest part can be remembering that other players' suburbs can impact your own scoring/money/population/etc.  But that's also part of the appeal.

RATING: 8
More tactical than strategic, this is nevertheless the most interesting city builder I've played, including things like Sim City and Cities Skylines on PC. It's much less of a sandbox than those PC games - there's a real competitive game to enjoy here. Also I love the aesthetic, a kind of modernist minimalism but with a splash more colour than that description may imply. I find that euros often suffer from visual busyness or an off-putting cartoonish look, but the tone set by the artwork here is just right.
There's also a pleasing amount of player interaction here - besides the familiar 'hate drafting', players can exploit each others' building choices (my rivals build a lot of residential areas so I start a homeowners' association to rake in the cash, for instance).

RATING: 9
Wonderfully-designed, tile-placement game I show people after Catan or Ticket to Ride because the strategy and the gameplay is very simple to grasp. Turns only involve one action and the mechanics of "activating" tiles are plain and easy to understand. For me, the best part of the game is the "catch" to "growing your population"/gaining points: it affects your income and the points you'll score next turn. Also, I love the constant weighing of public and private goals; it only slightly affects the amount of AP in the game, but it provides randomness and intrigue in a game that would be pretty dull without it. And the expansions definitely add to the variety of the base game, not get in the way of what made it so fun in the first place. Always a pleasure to play Suburbia.

RATING: 8
This is a mid-level Gamers' game about building out a Suburb/City. This is an excellent city/tableau building game that has immense depth and re-playability yet is able to be won by a first timer. The game is so smooth in that it teaches and play extremely well with little effort. Once a new player grasp the concept of how the tiles effect each other they can just run with it, and we have had several first time players win. The Goals in the game give players, especially new players a direction in to which they should start building their Suburb. This really eases the learning curve and early decision analysis paralysis of players because there is only a limited amount of choices each turn but endless combinations.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This turns out to be a simple drafting game. 6 tiles in the draft, each tile costing less the longer it's been in the draft as the tiles before it get taken and they shuffle along to the right. The tiles make the game, or rather the decisions you make on which tiles to collect win the game. Or even more rather, getting lucky when the tiles you've collected to date are supplemented later in the game by combos and they're not the ones that have been discarded pre-game. Which makes the result seem more like a gambling game than a drafting game. The tiles have all sorts of info to take into account - do you want more income (almost undoubtedly), instant VP's, each-turn VPs, combos which earn you money or VPs now or later. And so on and so on. Book-keeping gala. Hope your combos come. And hope no one takes them before you. As such, the decisions throughout the game are engaging, and there's replay on offer as you explore the combos available, but in the end, it's just a tile drafting game where you're playing in hope rather than expectation.

RATING: 6
"Very clever, but has too many types of building effects, which means there is too much to track."

What I like:
- Nice theme; minimal screwage
- Pretty good graphic design and components
- Only took 75 minutes, which was quicker than expected

What I don't like:
- Way too much to track, across multiple boards
- Too many different kinds of tile scoring
- Heavy reliance on majority scoring
- Non-intuitive mechanisms make learning games tough
- Scoring track really needs directional arrows

Overall, it's a near miss for me. I don't like games with so many different things interacting with other different things. This is especially bad in that some things are adjacent, some are within your board, and some are game-wide. Plus some are "what something else is adjacent to". It's all just a bit much.

I agree with the 2.7 weight.

RATING: 7.5
Based on one play: One of those games where you build up your area by buying tiles and as it gets bigger tiles tend to work together to get you more and more points or money. In this one, the tiles move your income or point track up and down which determines how many points you get each turn. Where it gets interesting is that there are also objectives that give bonus points. Each player has their own hidden one, but four are visible to everyone and they are worth a lot, so you must build up your area while also fighting for those objectives.

RATING: 7
Medium weight game with a city building theme.  Uses tile laying mechanics.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Suburbia is a city-building game in which players are drafting hex tiles to place in their personal play area.  The hexes will provide some sort of benefit, and may also help the players compete for one of the public or personal end-game goals.  Tiles may have effects that trigger based on the tiles next to them, the tiles on that player's board, or tiles in any players' boards.  The interesting thing is, tiles will have an immediate effect for any qualifying tiles when they are placed, but will also have continuing effects for subsequent qualifying tile placements.  This game has some interesting ideas, and you're definitely forced to pay attention to what everyone else is doing, but because of the tile draft, you don't end up having that much control over what ends up in your play area.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 7
Super nice components and solid gameplay makes this game a good choice. It was a bit mathy, but I think it was more that you had to keep track of so many things that it made it more laborious than it should have been. It played relatively quick and I am unsure of what I think about the goals.

I do think if all players are actively working towards the goals, the game plays much smoother. If anyone starts slacking, giving the goals to other players will literally cost you the game. Also, not really fond of the investment markers. They work, but barely. You have to back track on tiles if you apply it to such a tile and it can be difficult/impossible to remember the effects of it. Still, I rather like the game and would easily play more of it when asked.

RATING: 8.5
A very satisfying game, where accomplishing goals and building an engine of economy and reputation is always a balancing act. Figuring out that balance is a moderate brain-burner, rewarding the player who thinks with the big picture in mind. It took a few plays to really understand the fine tuning of the borough, combining multiple tiles to get quite beautiful combinations. Easy to learn and more enjoyable after multiple plays. RATING: 10
The choice points in this are brilliant. You need money and population, but there are always multiple ways to get them. Get cash now or build income? Get instant population, build reputation, or aim for goals? In any given play through the game, you need to do a little bit of each of these.
Since the line-up is random, though, the game doesn't grind to a halt as you try to puzzle out a solution. Each turn is pretty short.

RATING: 5
Overall a nice game, but it caters too much to the casual gamer for me. Your borough at the end of the game is rather small, you get the feeling you didn't get to do much in the entire game. The choices you face when thinking of purchasing tiles are rather similar: nothing is quite really bad to buy or good with the exception of the "Waterfront" tile. This is an obviously overpowered tile that breaks the 2 player game experience and gives a strong advantage to its owner on 3-4 players. The hidden objectives of this game are an afterthought (see thank you section in instruction manual) which should not have been integrated in the game since this is not a deduction game (out of 20 objectives you will probably never guess right and players have no real penalty in not accomplishing their secret goal). Finally, playing only a certain quantity of tiles is great for "a different game each time" but really hinders strategy since you may be building a borough with a certain strategy in mind which you will later find out was not even going to be playable since your synergy tiles were in the tiles that were not even played in the game. I was completely charmed by the potential of this game and the girlfriend loves the theme, but it is too short with only two phases (build income, then build reputation - end game) and there is too much luck for it to be a real strategy game. I am not sure what Mensa found so interesting in this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
P: 1-4, T: 90 min
Probably the best SimCity vibe game currently. Tile placement city builder, mid-weight Euro (engine building). Tile placement follows various set bonus scoring, some are coattail scoring. There are several levels of combos triggered off of tile placement. Ultimately its about zoning optimization. Mechanics enhance the theme

Personal and collective variable point scoring goals. Thus multiple paths to victory. Plays well with all player counts, solo game available.

-Tile artwork, while clean, is a little drab. There can be quite a bit of bookkeeping each turn, it can be easy to forget some triggers when placing tiles. For a mid weight game, the turns are quick, but it can play a little long.

Compared to Between Two Cities: a heavier, more strategic, variable and competitive Euro.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Quick and simple to play city building game, that gives you a lot of interesting choices and synergy between tile placements. It's fun when your tiles get you benefits from things other players have on their boards.

I like this a lot, but I'm not sure how I feel about the mechanic of only playing with about half the tiles each game. You might see a tile that gives +1 income per airport built from now on and then never have any airports come up, just out of randomness. Also, the graphic design is more than a bit dry. When this game was first released I didn't pick it up just because I didn't like the look of it. Overall the game-play won me over.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 8
The variety of tile interaction and the sliding market make for a game where your strategy will fall apart if you aren't able to rapidly shift your tactics. You have to build up both economies of income and reputation without growing so fast you burn out, or so slow you never get hot. You have to know when to pull in money and when to dump it all and try and push your population growth as fast and hard as possible. 

A game where every tile interacts with other tiles, often retroactively, requiring a certain amount of tracking, but allows for great combinations.

One thing I really like about this game, even moreso than in the sequel 'The Castles of Mad King Ludwig' is that by game's end, you have a really funky little city with its own story.

It looks nice and give a thematic feeling for city growth.
It does have a lot of downtime, because people have to review what is in the tile draft when their turn comes around, to try to optimize their purchase

RATING: 7
Only played this on iOS, but think there is a lot to like here. In real life a good half the time spent playing the game was just incrementing income, reputation, and population for every player. It's still a good game and is fun, but I think the app gives a better experience than the physical version.

RATING: 7
First of all, it's a very fine game. Lots of replayability in the box. Being mostly tactical, it's rarely predictable and always forces you to stay on your toes. The spatial element is interesting and makes you think about the shape of your future city (even more so with the "Suburcia INC." expansion). Also, it's satisfying to observe your little city grow. All of it makes "Suburbia" an enjoyable experience.

I have to say though, I did think I will like it more. It doesn't have a lot of that SimCity feel to me, most of the tiles are either "profit" or "reputation", rest doesn't matter. But that's the game in essence: balancing two factors, your money and reputation. It's definitely more "mathy" than "buildy", for lack of better word. In the end, the city looks like a bunch of random, colorful blobs, doesn't have that epic look of, say, "Carcassonne".

RATING: 6.86
I've played Suburbia now with a friend and with my wife and it's a fun game.  I definitely prefer this one to Ginkgopolis, but both are still fun.  I was afraid when I first saw the boards that this might use an auction mechanic, but it does not - it actually works out more like Morels with things you can buy shifting down over time and forcing you to pay more to take something when it is first revealed.  If shifting tiles down in a conveyor-belt manner bothers you, this might not be your game.  Other than that bit of fiddliness, it's a great Euro and one that I think does help avoid the old "I need more blues" or "you have more yellow than me"... instead you hear people saying things like "I think I'll build an elementary school to bring in more people" or "This parking lot will really help my income!"

RATING: 8
A good, not overly complex middle-weight euro. 
Based on my limited experience I don't really like tile placement as a core mechanic, but here it is incorporated into a bit of engine building, a bit of secret objectives and so it suits my tastes much more. I really love the arc the game tries to tell you: first you try to build up your economy to support the growth in your suburb then you try to increase your reputation (and parellel to that your population) while trying to reduce the negative impact of the population growth. Based on the two games I played you did your job well, when you finish the game with your economics falling apart but with just enough money to finish your plans.
I like the secret objectives and can see, how two experienced gamers can start to anticipate what the others' hidden objective could be. 
On the negative side, I don't know if the airport/restaurant/skycraper strategy would be efficient and if you add more tiles (with the expansions) their effectiveness dilute even more.
Currently I lack good middle weight euros (alongside the likes of Castles of Burgundy) so this is definetely a keeper in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The mechanics here feel very reminiscent of many other games I've played, but Suburbia's greatest strength might come in it's streamlined design. Basically, you build your town/engine up for the first half of the game, then you flip the switch and see who can acquire the highest population in the uncertain amount of time that's left over. Watch the end game conditions closely. Perhaps the only thing missing from the game is some token/counters to place on tiles that active constantly throughout the game. There were quite a few times where I discovered I'd missed something a turn or two afterwards, and it's nearly impossible to re-calculate everything if you need to.

Edit: Upon more plays, I've also realized the flaw in the end game goal tokens. It's entirely possible to receive a goal that contradicts another goal. Definitely creates a small disadvantage for that player.

Overall: Very enjoyable, but a few easy tweaks could improve it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an economic builder with echoes of Carcassonne, St. Petersburg, and Vegas Showdown. It has simple rules but offers lots to think about, and lots of nice combinations are possible. On the downside, there are a variety of ways that tiles interact with other tiles, not only in your own tableau but also in your opponents'. That's not a bad thing, but it's difficult to track. We have made quite a few mistakes by forgetting that when HE adds a residential tile, SHE gets $2, and so on.

Not at its best with two players, I think. Haven't tried it with more, but with two some of the tiles and goals seem quite overpowered. Having more players would smooth that out.

RATING: 8
I immediately saw some similarities between Suburbia and The Castles of Mad King Ludwig.  It has a similar feel.  You can tell it's from the same designer.  If you like one you will probably like the other.  I like Suburbia quite a bit more.

Perhaps my biggest reserve is the scoring mechanisms in these games seem too complicated.  I think they are just a bit too fiddly.  You will find yourself going through so many checks each time you place a tile just to verify how everything can be adjusted.  It's not too hard.  It's not enough for me to avoid the game.  It's just not...well, streamlined.  Not elegant.

Still fun!  :)

RATING: 6.5
I only played the Android application of this game (which works really well btw), so choose to ignore this comment.

Suburbia is a very pleasant tile laying game with a well-integrated theme You need a game or two to get how you should balance population growth and income. With a little experience most decisions are pretty much straight forward, which allows for smooth play.

However, there this is not a very deep game, but has a ton of bookkeeping. If I have the opportunity, I will give it a try though.

Update: Habe played it a physical game finally. As I expected there is a lot of bookkeeping and probably  some errors were done, but the game still was playing smoothly. In total, a good game.

RATING: 9.12
Easy to understand game play that just makes sense.  Not quite Sim City in board game form  as it's not that complex (i.e there isn't utility management for example).  However, as a board game it truly makes you think and plan. It just great to watch your borough expand and there is a real feel of building something.   There is player interaction with a many tiles that make you keep your eye on your opponents borough especially if you need to get rid of one; however, there isn't in-your-face aggression against each other.  The nastiest part is when you decide to play a little defense and pay to remove or turn a tile into a lake that your opponent could use.   Money it truly tight at first and if you don't create a good money engine early then you will struggle.  If I had to compare it to something, it has the building aspects of Glen More with the end conditions similar to Kingdom Builder, however, there is not only public ending condition point there are secret agent end of game points, just perfect for this game.  Just a spectacular game that is easy to learn and has good strategic and tactical decisions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Current players criticize classic roll & move games for intrinsically random (unfairly) and too samey (fairly). In twenty years the multiplayer solitaire games like this will be viewed as intrinsicly fiddly and error-prone (unfairly) and samey (fairly). 
Some designers are clearly stuck in rut (driven by a vocal section of players) of creating solitaire puzzles. In this case buying tiles which either earn income or victory points and which interact with each other (+1 X or Y per tile of Z type which is adjacent/in your town/on the board). The basic strategic dynamic is establishing early income and knowing when to switch to generating victory points (and of course setting up that switch). but the flaw/feature is that where-as a game like Dominion does this elegantly Suburbia requires the tracking of a host of potential interactions. If you enjoy mastering the fiddly rules then that is a selling point, but if not you will find it is too easy to miss something or make an error and as other players are not tracking your personal little board this could substantially alter the outcome of the game (did you really earn that win or did some combination of errors hand it to you?).

RATING: 9
I'm really enjoying this game. Tile laying game that feels a lot like Vegas Showdown in gameplay but without the auction. More player interaction between tiles and competing for goals, but this does require some time spent keeping track of tiles you have that may score when other tiles are played in the future (I like to put a $1 coin on those tiles and just go through them every time I or another player plays a tile). Thematically, you do feel like your building a city and deciding whether you will have lots of industry or residential or lakes etc, although perhaps you don't get the same nice aesthetic you get from a finished Carcassonne board. Probably best as a 3p game, but works quite well 2p (which Vegas Showdown does not) and the solitaire game is quite good with the bot rules (search for the Jule variant for more challenge).  I haven't tried 4p yet - there might be a bit too much book-keeping and you might feel more screwed by the luck of the draw - (in the 2nd half of the game, it seems like players are often buying the new tile that just came out on the far left of the track, so there is a large luck of the draw factor if the right tile come up for you). It would be cool if you could pay a little (like $2) to look at the top face-down tile in the current stack and can then buy it instead of another tile for $10 + the tile cost (or if you don't want it or can't afford it, put it back on the stack and buy a different tile for the normal cost) - I might try this variant.

RATING: 8.9
I usually wait for quite a few plays before rating a game, but I am doing a bunch of ratings at the moment. Nevertheless I have played 6 games, the last 4 with Inc expansion, which I already think greatly improves it and is very important for the ultimately replayability.
By the way, just out of interest, I came across Galaxy Trucker and Suburbia as a result of studying the highly rated games by Race for the Galaxy devotees (ie 10/10), as I also consider RFTG as possibly the very best game, but do wish to maintain variety in gaming. This approach seems to have worked.
20170326; Am decreasing rating .1, because, after now playing perhaps 12 or so games, I am convinced that the goals are too highly valued such that they over-ride most other considerations. If all the goals were worth 5 less, I think it would make a lot of difference, allowing at least some consideration of possible powerful moves which may slightly compensate the loss of a goal. I don't think we have had a single win from anyone who did not score the most goals - the best we have had is to be within a few points of the highest goal scorer. And this not infrequently causes a degeneration into a goal race, such as the game today where for the most part we were simply racing to pickup greens and borders as goals. My opponent had opportunities for clever play on his last turn, but simply had to pick up a lake border on the last turn as losing the goal to me was worth 2 or 3 points more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
A solid game. Simple, to-the-point in mechanics, Analytical in its gameplay. 

The game is a tableau-builder with simple market mechanic. Each round, you pay for one tile from the market that gets cheaper the farther along it stays on the market. It is not only easy to deny people of their choice pieces, it may benefit you well if timed properly as you flip the piece on its back side to place a lake in your borough. 

The game rewards long-term planning, having clear phases where you first develop your engine and subsequently transit your purchases to point generation. The trick then is to find that balance, and knowing when to step on the gas without hurting yourself at the end. 

Would like to try with Suburbia Inc. next time.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
+ Can be fun to figure out the optimal position and timing for the developments in your suburb. 
+ The variety of goals encourage players to explore a variety of strategies, giving you slightly different things to think about with every game. 

- The goals sometimes come out in combinations that force the gameplay to become distorted and boring (like getting into a lake-building race). 
- Private goals sometimes cause large, unexpected swings during endgame scoring, and as a result I usually feel blasé no matter what the final outcome is.
- Every turn, there can be a lot of bookkeeping and a lot of different tiles to check, but it becomes somewhat more routine with repeated plays.
- Artwork is utilitarian and rather cold.

RATING: 9
The perfect game for a lazy Sunday afternoon, played with a friend or two.

Only played two 3-player games so far, each time with new people. The game is very easy to grasp, while it takes sometime to get accustomed to all the symbols - people seem to confuse income with money and sometimes miscalculate the usefulness of some tiles - but all that comes with knowing the game and knowing what kind of tiles are going to be available later on (possibly available - as not all tiles are ever used).

I also very much like the goals (public and personal) and feel like this really makes the game. People are trying to beat each other for the bonus points, which is where the actual competition comes into play - in what is otherwise a very relaxed and non-confrontational tile laying game.

So far played with casual/non-gamers as well as proper gamer gamers, both parties have liked the game in equal measures, there is enough interesting decisions to make, and the complexity is somewhat hidden to not intimidate the casual gamers.

Update 1:
Now played a few more games, including several 2 player games (not played with 4 players yet). Even with just two players the game retains its interesting qualities. The goals are slightly easier to control and the market place is more predictable as there is only 1 person to compete against, but I don't feel like these hurt the base game that much. Prefer it with more players, but it is my go-to 2-player game at this point.
