RATING: 6
Simple drafting of city tiles that complement each other well and produce a good income flow early and points later on. It's fiddly as you add your tiles checking for all the points, income and reputation fluctuations dependent on other tiles. Easy enough to pick up and play quickly, yet it doesn't feel that tense even when you're competing head to head with another player for big bonus tiles. After a few turns, when your city is starting to really take form, the drafting seems to be a bit obvious as to what you should buy or bury (by turning into a lake or purchasing a base tile). It's all designed so that games should be close. Fun theme. It's okay, but not very exciting when it comes down to it. I think I prefer Irondale in the city building genre for a bit more interaction and choice, and Pax Porfiriana or Civilization: Through The Ages for tough card drafting choices. Not a bad way to spend an hour though.

Suburbia, City Tycoon, Urban Sprawl, Ginkgopolis, Urbania... so many city building games in such a short time. None of them seem to have quite cracked it yet, but I've got a few more to try.

------------------

A definite improvement with further play. There's more interaction that I thought at first. The tile drafting costs mesh together well with the income focused beginning moving to VP generation timing. Money is not so hard to come by (which is good), and so you see buildings of all costs and drafting positions being bought and buried.

My main complaint is the points seem to be very evenly matched, except the bonus tiles, where the difference between one player drawing a 15 pointer and another a 20 pointer is enough to decide the game.

RATING: 6.7
I enjoy the game but two things don't quite live up to my expectations: (1) The feeling of accomplishment of building a city is diminished because the graphics on the tiles are primarily devoted to giving game information while the city-scape graphics are very simple, washed out, and very much a secondary consideration. (2) The primary focus of the game is finding the right balance between population and economy which makes building a beautiful or sensibly designed city unimportant and often pretty much impossible. The players are thinking first and foremost about what tile and placement are going to give the best results to their population and economy tracks. What makes a pleasing city doesn't really enter into the equation.

RATING: 4
It's a MPS puzzly thingy of sorta tableau building style (tiles, cards - same thing). Stuff you've seen before: public market of tiles (new ones being more expensive), individual player boards, goals (common and individual), getting many points in many different ways. It doesn't move as fast as drafting games, but it's in that general league with 7 Wonders and Amongst the Stars. 

But makes 1 thing better than all the competition: narrative. Not only it's pretty good in building yourself something genre (and relatively short). It also makes for interesting stories if you think musings about social classes and urbanism are fun (tee hee hee): [i]hey I've build retirement village next to fast food and population skyrocketed. [/i] Or putting that social housing project in the far away part of town, behind the large lake. 

EDIT - in hindsight I like the theme and general flow of the game, but find some parts too gamey and needlessly complicated.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An alright city-building game which is much simpler and more thematic than its bureaucratic appearance lets on. At its core, it's all about optimising the purchase and placement of the right tiles. There are, however, public and secret goals which will influence your choice of tiles. The "speed bumps" along the VP track also require players to build up a steady income before powering through the bumps towards the end.

While I don't have any major issues with the game, there are several nitpicks which gradually add up. Most prominently, it gets increasingly fiddly to optimise remaining moves while keeping track of building effects to be triggered. There's also the minor snag of luck in the tile draws – buildings which depend on specific tiles appearing (airports, offices and the like) can be notoriously underpowered if the right tiles don't appear in the pool.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
This was on my list to buy, but I probably won't now that I have played it. It's not because I don't like the game, because I REALLY do like it. Like Legacy: Gears of Time, it has simple mechanisms, but lots of strategic game play. HOWEVER, the constant bookkeeping necessary while playing is VERY distracting. We had a 5th person doing nothing but that during a 4 player game and it still took a couple hours to play. I have decided to wait for the iOS version of Suburbia that has been announced to get and play since it will handle all of the bookkeeping and enable the players to focus more on the actual game. Plus, there have been some production issues with the printed version of the game evident in the copy I was playing and that bothers me, especially at that price point.

I have played Suburbia several times and it's fun, but I could see it feeling samey after a bit. It's a neat game overall, solo play is a bonus, but the tiles are a cut a bit off-centered in every copy I've seen (this kind of thing bothers me), there is a lot of fiddly bookkeeping that's necessary during the game, and even though there are various strategies, it isn't overly deep. It does a good job of replicating the SimCity feel and I like it, but I recommend that people try it before buying, if possible, because I'm not quite sure it's worth the money. They already have an expansion in the works that will add some new mechanisms to the game, but I'm not sure it's necessary. I think I'm more excited about getting and playing the iOS version of the game once it gets released rather than owning the physical board game.

RATING: 8.5
Deceptively deep! This game has a lot going on in terms of strategies you can pursue and how the scoring pans out. Some people feel the end game goals are too 'swingy' - they are, but this is a factor that a good player will take into account by assuming their opponents have goals which can gain them 20 points and planning around this by chasing the public goals and ensuring that their own private goals are met.

The expansion, Suburbia Inc adds a fun element of direction in terms of new challenges to accomplish as adding borders which alter how your tableau develops. I am a fan of both tile laying and tableau building,and find Suburbia an excellent implementation of both mechanics. A very good game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think this is a fun city-building game, but it has two major flaws:

1.) The buildings Casino and PR-Firm are overpowered if they come out early in the game. They should be removed, as they meddle with the inherent balancing mechanisms of Suburbia.

2.) The (personal) goals are much too important. Often the luck of the draw will simply prevent you from achieving a goal. Goals are almost always game deciding, due to the huge amount of points they provide. If all players simply need to fulfill their personal goals to be able to win, then they are poorly designed. I personally like the idea of goals but the amount of points they provide needs to be toned down a lot.

Update: After eagerly anticipating The Castles of Mad King Ludwig by the same designer, it seems that it has the exact same problem with the personal goals (maybe even more so), especially for low player counts ...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[2017-03-19]
A King Ludwig óta nagyon vártam, hogy kipróbálhassam az elődöt, mert a témája sokkal közelebb állt hozzám, mint az utódé és nem is csalódtam. Várost építünk, lakó, üzleti és ipari negyedekkel, amiket parkokkal, kikapcsolódási zónákkal színesítünk. Célunk, hogy minél többen jöjjenek hozzánk és lakjanak nálunk, ehhez pedig körönkénti bevételünket is szinten kell tartani, hisz ebből építjük az épületeket. Körönként a már több játékban ismert mechanizmussal tudunk lapkát szerezni, azaz elsők olcsóbbak, a következők plusz pénzbe kerülnek. Pénzhiány esetén a szerzett épületek tóvá alakíthatók, amik így ingyen vehetők el és azonnali pénzt hoznak. Kőkemény euró létére nagyon tematikus a játék. Az épületek egymásra hatnak a játék közben, amire nem könnyű a figyelés, mert játékostársaink épületei által is változhatnak a városunk értékei. A játékban van 4 nyílvános cél, ami a játék végén 10-20 lakót hoz a városnak és mindenki a játék elején két-két titkos célból választ. Én kicsit soknak tartom az ez által szerezhető laókat. Így azért erre különösen érdemes figyelni. Nagyon tematikus, hogy ahogy egyre többen laknak a városunkban úgy egyre nehezebb új városlakókat magunkhoz csábítani és a fenntartási költségeink is nőnek. A játék vége nem fix, ez általában nem tetszik de jól működik. Első játékomban teljesen más stratégiák mentén a három újonc 8 ponton belül végzett. Kicsit talán lehetett volna szebb a grafika. Elsőre erős 7-es, ha sikerül még játszanom vele emelkedhet a pontszám.

RATING: 8
Suburbia is an excellent tile-placement, city building game with loads of tough decisions and tactical strategy. The rules, though, are quite simple. You take turns drafting tiles to put into your city, trying to get the best reputation and lure people into your little hamlet. While the game scales fairly well, I prefer it with two. Going through the effects of each tile can get quite overwhelming the larger your town gets, particularly if you have to check the towns of three other players. There are also a couple of tiles that are a bit too strong for my liking (the Casino & PR Firm), but there is enough variety to just remove them. Overall, Suburbia is a great game for those that are just stepping past the beginner or gateway level games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great city-building euro.

The artwork is a bit bland but I love the thematic nature of the different city-tiles. They all work as you would imagine.

The scoreboard has a wonderful feature that, when your cross one of red lines, your income and reputation both drop by 1, representing the increase of bureacracy in your city. You need to be more and more effecient to keep yout city going.

Also, I love how easy it is to teach. The rulebook is short, everything makes sense, and that makes it uneccessary to re-read the rules if you haven't played in a long time.

I have only played the game with 2-3 players and I fear that you would loose to much control with which tiles are avaiable to you with 4.


I have one gripe with game though: The goals. First of all I'm not sure they are totally balanced (although I believe it's more pronoucned in The Castles of Mad King Ludwig). Secondly and even more annoying, are the public goals. If you play a 3 player game, the goals that give you VP's for having less tiles of a specfific color than the other players, leads to a sort of king making. 

Let me explain:
A nonspecific public goal in a given game gives a player 15 VP's if they have the fewest yellow tiles in their borough at the end of the game. In a 3 player game it now becomes a race for whoever gets a yellow tile first, so he or she has one more than the others. Why? Because now the other players cannot aqquire any yellow tiles unless they want to give 15 points to the remaining player. maybe it's a case of group-think, but it just rubs me the wrong way.

Suburbia is a board game version of SimCity. For that reason I enjoy it more than I would otherwise. On the other, there are several opportunities for parasitism and screwing your neighbour. I don't like suburban development at all, but I like Suburbia. See also Castles of Mad King Ludwig, which I might like a bit more.

RATING: 6.5
This is a tile-laying city-building economic game for 2-4. It's sort of SimCity, in board game form (but no monsters - yet)! The rules are very thematic, so that you end up designing a city that more or less makes sense and fits with what you expect a city to look like. Thanks to varying end game bonuses (objectives) and the fact that only a subset of tiles are used each game, every game should be different. 

Game play is fun, but there are several annoying elements:
- having to count the number of specific tiles in play in ANY players' cities
- having to track everyone's progress towards all the objectives
- some buildings, like the PR Firm and the Casino, seem VERY powerful (overly so)
- the way that the (semi)randomly distributed goals and face up goals sometimes get distributed (e.g., some people facing incompatible goals, or goals that happen to match perfectly) 

I think I'll like it better with fewer players; and probably best on the iPad!

--- Summary ---
 The central board has:
- 7 face-up tiles in a row which, in Showmanager style, are available for purchase by paying the tile's base cost plus an additional cost that rises as you get further back in the line. 
- several additional copies of the starting tiles, also available for purchase (if bought, the player still discards one of the 7 face-up tiles)
- 3 groups of tiles (A, B, C), which are the Stock from which to replenish the supply line of tiles. They are also the game timer: an end-of-game tile is shuffled into the bottom portion of the C deck. When this tile comes up, finish that round AND ONE MORE, then the game ends.
- several end-game objective tiles, chosen at random (the single player achieving each objective will score the indicated VP)
Each player starts with
- the same set of tiles, creating a little city with income (cash earned each turn) and reputation (population, or VP, earned each turn)
- cash
- 2 personal, secret goal tiles (for end-game VP): after seeing the public objectives, keep ONE of them

On your turn, buy a tile (you do have the option to reduce the base cost to $0 if you buy it face down, as water) and place it in your city. Then, apply the effects printed on the tile you placed AND any other related tile on your or any other players' boards. Tiles may give you cash (e.g., earn $2 when some tiles are adjacent to water), or modify your income or reputation; or they may modify the effect of other tiles (e.g., every restaurant in play increases the income bonus of every farm in play by $1). Income and reputation are tracked on your personal board. 

At the end of your turn, gain/lose cash (based on income) and population (based on reputation, and tracked on the score board). As your pop increases, you'll cross one or more red lines; for each line crossed, you must reduce your income and rep by 1. Replenish the tiles available for purchase by sliding them to fill in the gap, and adding a new tile from the (A, B, or C) stack of city tiles to the back of the line.

At the end of the game, players earn VP for:
- goals: the public objectives and their private objective, if they are the ONLY player to achieve the goal.
- money: each $5 buys 1 pop
Most VP wins (tiebreakers: rep, then income, then remaining cash)!

RATING: 8
I went back and forth quite a bit in my head about this game.  The initial press blurbs and tidbits I heard about this game intrigued me.  "Sim City as a board game" was the biggest one that piqued my interest.

Then I read a full review in Spielbox about this game and my interest waned quite a bit.  It was a positive review but the way they portrayed it made it seem a little too light for me.

Then a close friend of mine showed up to game night with his brand new copy and I had to find out first-hand, and I'm glad I did because it completely changed my mind back.  This is a very fun and very well-designed game.  It is certainly on the lighter side of things, but in all the right ways.  It isn't overly convoluted and it is certainly easy and quick to teach new players.  But there's also a decent amount of variety both in the tiles and the game mechanics that there is a lot of strategic and tactical thinking to consider.  I also love the mechanic of having a hidden private goal to strive for in addition to the public goals everyone has knowledge about.

I got demolished my first game because I tried to play the system and completely miscalculated.  Building up income and money early and filling my town with the tiles necessary to take the majority of the goals and then switching gears to focus entirely on population (this game's 'victory points') when I thought it was the "end-game".  Unfortunately the game lasted quite a few more rounds than I expected and I found myself with no income or money to buy the expensive end-game buildings and the mechanic of crossing red lines that lower both income and reputation meant my end-game sprint resulted in me not collecting population consistently any more either.

There's a lot of interaction between the tiles which I love, you can even interact with tiles in other players' burroughs.  And the interactions make sense thematically.  For example, a slaughterhouse lowers your reputation by quite a bit but gives you more income based on the number of restaurants nearby.  This is actually quite a thematic game overall and the different mechanics make logical sense and are original and fun, like the above mentioned "red lines".  As your city grows in population you lose income for the upkeep.  The loss of reputation they describe as losing "small town" status and is a little wonky but I guess the theme can't be completely perfect.

My only complaint is the same as with any game like this, where most of the gameplay comes from the interaction of the different elements, the city tiles in this case.  With a finite number of tiles, eventually the best ones and the best combos become apparent and games devolve into competition for those best lines of play (which can sometimes be way too dependent on "luck of the draw"). Although expansions are assuredly planned for this game to extend its life.  It can also become a bit difficult to remember in all the ways you need to make adjustments when a new tile is added later in the game.

RATING: N/A
In Suburbia players play some kind of board Sim City. There are yellow (industrial), grey (cultural), green (residential) and blue (comercial) types of buildings. There are also lakes, that work more like a plan B action. All of these tiles appear on gameplay divided in 3 decks (A, B and C) thus trying to make some sense of evolution, and they are bought by players and placed in player's displays, forming a pattern of hexagons on different colors that are supposed to represent blocks of certain activities. In the end, what players want is to increase population on the city and accomplish some goals (having more money, being the player with more or less income, have more residential/cultural/comercial buildings), you name it! Every goal and its opposite should be in the game. All of this is very, or all, more than abstract.
The action mechanism is quiet simple and that is an advantage for the game. Players select one tile from a row (the more the tile is new the most expensive it gets) and apply it into his city activating it. The activation is made with symbols that represent population, income and popularity. This representation is color coded and simple to understand too but still only a pattern of tiles.
And players go round after round, buying tiles (buildings) that when placed closed to others give more or less benefits, depending on each one's abilities. Simple but also boring. And also boring is the non-sense of grow when displaying a tile. I had no such feeling of growth. Even when you get more advantages depending on a number of 'green tiles' you have on your display, or 2 money for each 'grey tile', that's not enough for the city building feeling, in my opinion.
In the end, this isn't a bad game. But it isn't a very good one too. Because it had so much more potential and it could be so much more interesting to play. And it has one of those lazy solutions on scoring (bonus tiles) that are related to everything you come to do in the game, even some that you didn't choose to: the player with the most income can win 15VP's in the end, but so can the player with the least income, for example. And these bonus tiles, in the end, can mean more than 30% of all the hard work one had during play. I can play it occasionally.

(I)

This makes it an extremely fun game each and every time I play it.  I especially like that you will only see on average about half the tiles in any given stack every time you play. Once I start getting some better competition and lose a game or two, I'm sure it will become a 10.  However, if I continue to win with ease, it may drop as my interest in playing drops.

EDIT:  yup, the latter. I'll still play this if people request it, but it's not as fun as it once was because I seem to always win. I may have to see if adding the expansion helps.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Purchase tiles from a Through the Ages-style row and add them to your city, getting benefits from the tile itself as well as those adjacent or even in other players' boroughs. It's all very thematic, if blandly illustrated: if you build a heavy factory you increase your income, but you don't want housing adjacent to your industrial zone or you'll lose reputation. Airports go up in value with each new airport built by anyone. If you build a fancy restaurant, it's worth a lot at the start, but as competitors open up your income decreases. Many of the later buildings are marvelously cynical.

The degree and depth of these synergies makes the game more interesting than somewhat similar titles like Glen More. Much of the strategy comes from targeting the randomly-selected public and private goals (most houses, most reputation, most money etc.), which are worth significant points.

There's masses of interplay variability, with many tiles (both cities and goals) not included in the mix for any given game; the flipside is a very real luck factor. Hope you get the right building at the right time! Early decisions have very uncertain payoff: memorizing the tiles doesn't help as much as it could because not all tiles are included. That airport isn't such a good deal if none of the rest come out! Private goals can also be frustratingly out of your control. If you take the "fewest green buildings (+20)" goal and another player doesn't build a green at all, there is nothing you can possibly do to influence that. 

It's also a game that doesn't seem to gain much except time from adding players; the sweet spot is probably 2-3.

I enjoy the game but it offers just [i]slightly[/i] too little control to be a favourite.

Ratings history: 7 -> 5 (the game hasn't changed but I am now much less forgiving)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
As of this rating, this game is rated lower than Castles of Mad King Ludwig, which is almost criminal.

The great: It's thematically tight to an extent I haven't seen since Agricola. The minimalist design is visually appealing. The pieces minimize text effectively. The game moves briskly. The goal system rewards flexibility, and the cash/income system rewards strong planning and tactical soundness. The income-loss system helps prevent runaway leaders. The randomness of available buildings can significantly alter the tempo and decisions from game to game.

The not-so-great: It's very easy to miss something and lose track of your cash/income/reputation, which causes scoring issues to the extent that it's very challenging to score a full game correctly. The goals' outsized rewards can push the luck impact too high. The randomness in game end-time seems intended to prevent "timing" the endgame, but often leads to one or more players simply hoping for an early/late end to the game.

The verdict: I have never failed to enjoy a play of this game. It plays light but with depth, and appeals to a broad audience.

RATING: 7.5
Definitely feels like a city building game. It is fun and definitely has a good engine building system, but loses points for a lot of the fiddliness and clunkiness of some systems. Keeping track of all the different conditions is maintenance heavy and definitely detracts from the game. The losing income and reputation when passing certain population tracks is a bit double handed... it rewards building a long term engine over a short time gain. Not necessarily bad, but does feel like it limits some strategic paths.

Some improved suggestions would be: The tiles that have global bonuses need to be highlighted more. Additionally, the end game tile being shuffled into the last 9 makes the last turn fairly random, especially when a lot of the last tiles are so powerful and can turn a game around.

RATING: 7
Suburbia is basically Sim City: The Board Game. You get income from your city, buy buildings, and strategically add them to your town to maximize your profits and reputation.

It does get a little slow late-game. There's a plot of slow point calculation in this game--every tile you put down might affect every tile around it, and might get bonuses based on not just *your* board, but from tiles placed by ALL of the players. There's a lot of "Wait, how many residential tiles does everyone have?" and "How many restaurants do you have?" It's interestingly complex, but it does slow things down.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Suburbia is a tight game of city building that in many ways reminds me of a more elaborate version of a drafting game like [thing=66362]Glen More[/thing]. You have to choose a vision for your burgeoning suburb, and then try to fill it with synergistic buildings from a common tableau, all the while hoping that the other players don't choose the buildings you want. Of course like any game of its kind, keenly observing the other players is a must so that you know when to select a building that may not be ideal for your personal strategy, but that hurts the other player(s) enough to be worth the hate-draft.

As for what you are building, the joy of the game comes when you see your city grow before your eyes. It takes on a personality of its own depending on the type of buildings you install. Will you be an airport hub, a residential paradise, or a commercial powerhouse? All options. The game-play tension, however, comes in the form of balancing population growth (points) against income (money to buy future buildings). You can't have both, but of course you need both. The only way to overcome that typically zero-sum limitation is to look for positive interactions between the building you are buying and the buildings you (and your fellow players) have already built.

And this is where my criticism for the game comes in. If I fault the game anywhere it's in the elaborate checking and re-checking that you have to perform each round to see how your new building scores you bonuses. If you are playing with four people, for example, you not only need to check your own board, but also the boards of each opponent. This lends some much needed player interaction to the game, but it ultimately comes off as more chore than joy. While it can be satisfying to take advantage of someone else's hard work, those moments are less common than you might think. But still, it's easy enough to get into the habit. It doesn't interfere with the flow of the game so much as it create the feeling of a bookkeeping exercise.

I suppose my other criticism has to do with repeat plays. Because the game is quite good, it rewards experience. However, the game also falls into the trap of having set strategies that players can repeat over and over. This is mitigated by the random draw of tiles, but not by the actions of the other players. I would prefer the latter.

So overall, I find Suburbia a tight game that is also fun to play. The lack of true player interaction will prevent it from rising to the top of my ratings heap, but that doesn't mean I'm not impressed.

RATING: 5
Build a city, keeping costs low and influx of residents high, and managing the spatial relationships between different kinds of buildings.

Suburbia simplifies city management and development down to a few easy to manage resources (money, income, population, and immigration) and has a ton of variability. It can be a little to easy to forget what you and your opponents have built in the first few plays, but after a while it becomes second nature.

Brilliant iOS implementation.

A city building game accomplished via tile placement. The ruleset is pretty light. Overall, this is a pleasant game that isn't particularly demanding, but still offers some nice tactical depth. The random goals are a nice touch for replayability.

I suspect a 2 player game would allow for some added light strategy. In larger games, the tile selection for future turns is rather unpredictable.

I could see a common complaint regarding the bookkeeping, but for me it wasn't too bad. Though, everyone needs to pay attention or they might miss a benefit here or there.

A minor quibble I have is that the building specific artwork on the tiles is pretty bland.

I might have to lobby for this as a replacement for Alhambra down the road.

RATING: 10
I was sort of committed for a while to only thinking this game was ok.  I think it was just such a benign theme (I hate the suburbs in real life so why on earth would I want to play a game about one?).  But here's the deal: it's exceptional.  I think it might be one of the finest designs I've every played.  The relationship between income, reputation, and overall population (score) is complex and exciting and difficult to master even after a few plays.  And the interaction between tiles within and between player tableaus is first-rate.  I can't speak highly enough of this game.  Get it, give it the due time to wrap your head around, and enjoy yourself.  It's just so good.

RATING: 7
A simple and elegant urban planning game.

I love games with simple rules and easy mechanics that have great depth and replaybility. It allows me to set-up, teach and play the game easily and spend my time making critical decisions and enjoying the game. Suburbia fits exactly this description. The classic drafting is supported so well with tile placement and set collection - thanks to some great tile design - that the end result is impressive. The game flows well, has tough decisions, requires planning ahead and creates a great sense of accomplishment.

There is little to dislike here, other than the artwork and the general production value. The game deserves much more in presantation. Other than that, the randomness of the tiles puts me slightly off, as I'd prefer to know for sure how many of the key combo buildings will be available in the future. 

Overall it's a fine game, perfect for urban planners.

RATING: 10
What I like:

-The quintessential city building game - a theme most folks can get behind
-Hits the playing time sweet spot
-A great balance between complex and intuitive for both new and experienced players
-Fun management of your city's Growth versus economics
-The way the market works creates some very interesting decisions
-High replay value with the many different tiles and strategies

What I dislike
Having played way over 100 games of this I feel that the goals are actually a bit too important. Once you realise this, the game almost becomes a race of chicken to see who is willing to crash and burn harder to get the goals. The otherwise amazing amazing expansion only increases this as it makes it easier to get a great city even while only focusing on the goals. There is no winning just by creating the best city - you need the goals and preferably most of them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
After several more plays, my initial impressions are mostly still accurate.

A lot of very clean pieces fit together mechanically, and it's very easy to play through a game and make interesting decisions and have an enjoyable time.  But it's also easy for people to be rewarded disproportionately due to tiles coming up when they're in position.  If this was just a case of hitting what you're hoping to draw (and thus rewarding diversity of preparation), that would be fine. 

Unfortunately, a couple of cards (most notably the Casino and PR Firm) unfairly reward ANYONE who happens to be in position to snag them if they come up early enough in their respective stacks.  When either of these cards are grabbed early enough, they benefit ANY strategy, and provide WAY more money income/population income for the dollars spent than any other building, barring odd circumstances.  They are worth buying even from the most expensive position if they come up at the right time, and take no preparation/strategy aside from "have money to buy stuff".  They essentially unlock your scoring potential, which otherwise caps due to the red lines.

There may be other tiles that are also push the luck envelope a bit more than I care for, but those are definitely the egregious offenders, because they're no-brainer lucky.

I'm ok playing the game again, but not suggesting it myself.  And I might go so far as to suggest playing with house-ruled nerfs to the clearly broken tiles.

RATING: 8

If there is a lot of competitive tension in the group, this is a must get.

RATING: 8
A city building game that uses hex-shaped tiles to assemble your own neighborhood. Players take turns buying tiles before their opponents can purchase it for themselves. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This and Castles of Mad King Ludwig are similar, mechanically, and this is the one we end up playing most.  The theme is not necessarily as strong, but as someone with a degree in environmental/urban planning, I still love this game.

There are a lot of icons involved, but every tile also has text to help you make sense of things.  This is probably one reason we prefer it to Castles.  It's also pretty competitive and sometimes you'll be torn between doing things to help you get points during the game that may take away your points at the end of the game.  The red lines where you have to drop your population and reputation seemed like they might feel too punishing, but they usually don't.  It forces you to think about balance - if you score too big too fast you'll be scaled back.  This also helps keep one person from running away with the game and making everyone else feel like they should give up.

RATING: 2
Random Boredom and Stupidity in Suburbia

Why the hell are a large number of tiles randomly removed from the game?

For variety/replayability? Bollocks! None of the tiles are particularly interesting or unique and nobody gives a crap or even notices a slightly different tile mix from game to game. However, most if not all tiles of a certain type may randomly be removed and make other tiles worthless with no way for players to know this and leading different paths/strategies into a complete deadend. This is just plain stupid.

For unpredictability? Bollocks. There are several ways that tiles are taken/discarded so that you cannot rely on getting specific tiles, so it is completely unnecessary to randomly remove tiles from the game. That other players can screw you over intentionally or accidentally is of course just fine, but that the game setup can screw you over before even starting to play is just moronic game design.

Also, far too fiddly with a "simple" 7-step player aid checklist to go through all the admin everytime you place a tile - including looking for tiny, near invisible symbols on tiles in other players' tableaux when choosing/scoring tiles.

Also, very limited interaction - primarily taking/discarding tiles which other players may want but this is very limited as only a few tiles are affordable and it is not worth spending lots of money just to obstruct another player.

Finally, I did not even lose the game although I lost all interest less than halfway into the game and took crappy action after crappy action which just happened to fulfill my goal plus a common goal rushing past another player who actually played the game.

RATING: 4
Suburbia is a game I want to like. Not only is urban planning my profession but I also love games with a spatial element. Yet I find myself after two plays disinterested in Suburbia for the following reasons: (a) The focus on increasing income then victory points is too formulaic and one-dimensional. Sure, the details may vary, but in the end you're doing roughly the same thing every game. Fiddling around with a bunch of different tiles just to increase yourself on two linear tracks gets old fast (b) The goals rub me the wrong way. Having the "most" or "least" of something isn't very interesting nor really congruous with the rest of what's going on in the game, yet the goals are a huge chunk of scoring. To spend 90 mins drafting and spatially arranging tiles on your tableau, developing your economy and later your point engine, and then have the winner come down to who had the least cash on hand at game end just feels stupid. Not to mention each player's secret goal; how that adds value to the game experience other than making player scores non-calculable is beyond me.

To its credit, the game does not overstay its welcome, which excuses some of its one-dimensionality. Yet in the end the spatial elememt isn't interesting enough nor is it a big enough part of the game to make me want to revisit this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
So far I'm loving Suburbia. It really captures the spirit of SimCity while creating its own unique game system. It's loads of fun to try and figure out the best way to combo-out your tiles and to try and thwart your opponents with a well timed hate draft.

There has been some criticisms leveled at the end game bonus goal tiles for being an unbalancing factor. My take is that in a straight up pure point calculating analysis, they are incredibly over-powered. If someone grabs two public goals, they will most likely win...It doesn't matter how well you have planned your city. In-game scoring can not carry you to victory if someone else bonuses out at the end.

Does this break or weaken the game? In my opinion, no. It simply requires players to be a little more alert to what everyone else is doing. It requires everyone to play defense. If everyone is playing alertly, it becomes VERY difficult for someone to score multiple public goals. What we have here is the Puerto Rico effect. If someone at the table is inexperienced or of a significantly lower skill level, someone else will probably win by a landslide.

That's not really a problem in my group. I think as long as a new players are made aware of the importance of the goals going in, they can probably avoid King-making someone else.

Aside from that, I can't recommend this game enough as far as fun factor goes. I can't wait to get my grubby little paws on the expansion...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

Like a lot of games, it's not so much that I disliked it as that there are quite a few similar games that I'd rather play.

RATING: 6
A multi-player Sim City-like board game.  The player who makes the most populous city is the winner.  Fun concept and a good implementation, but with a significant caveat: it is largely multi-player solitaire, but requires players to keep a close eye on other people's turns. Negative goals are particularly annoying.  A good game, but not good enough of a game to warrant the focus it requires of me.

RATING: 7.5
I really like a lot of the driving mechanics of this game.  The spatial aspects of getting the most out of your tiles and the sliding scale of costs to acquire those tiles felt good and I liked how the various goals gave you something to focus on through the game.  However I did find the book keeping a little tedious. Counting all the different types of tiles that interacted across the cities could make it hard to see which purchase would be most beneficial... but trying to keep track of which of my tiles would change value or interact whenever anybody purchased a certain type of tile seemed too easy to mess up on.  I can see where a group all watching out for those instances could keep things flowing smoothly... but I think I'd prefer this as a computer game to keep track of those permutations.

All the same I'd like to play it again to solidify my opinion one way or the other as the spatial and engine building aspects were really neat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.7
Last week we were visiting one of the biggest European boardgamestores. While we were looking around in the store I noticed this game in the corner with damaged games. In my Essen 2013 recommendations, I gave this a thumbs down. Simcity converted into a boardgame doesn’t seem interesting to me at all. I loved Simcity and a boardgame of it, should be filled with tons of rules in my eyes. And with over 600 new games to look at in one day, you do a really rough selection. But, somehow, the game kept coming back to me. I’ve read some reviews about it, and slowly (very slowly) I became interested in this one. I the months between October and April, I didn’t became convinced to buy this game, but I sure wanted to give it a try. I even had looked it up, in some on-line stores, but with a price of about EUR50,-, the interest in it turned down very quickly. But there it was. A damaged copy of Suburbia. I took the box and I noticed that it was almost 50% off compared to the on-line stores. And the damage? There were two corners pressed a little (and I mean very little) bit, due to stacking boxes. Decided to by this one, and turn it into a ‘try after you buy’-game.

One day later, I started to unpunching the tiles, and at that point, the frustration began. It had to use a small knife, to cut the edges of the tiles, because the tiles weren’t cut as they should. Over 1 hour later, I finished the job, and I could start looking up the rules on the Internet. We bought a German copy of the game, and I prefer Dutch. I didn’t found any Dutch rules, so I downloaded the English rules (I prefer to read English above German, and M. does vice versa, so two rulebooks in two different languages isn’t to bad for us). Printed out a copy and started to read.

After reading the rules, which aren’t written that well, it seemed like a pretty easy game to us. On a players turn a player:


    Picks a tile to build and pay it’s costs and the extra costs indicated on the spots on the market
    Adjust their income and popularity tracks according to the effects triggered by the new placed tile. This means that all tiles in play (yours and the tiles of other players) have to be checked
    Collect money (indicated by the money track) and let your community grow (indicated by the popularity track)
    Move the tiles on the marked that are left of the just bought tile to the right, and add a new tile from the stack.

Especially step 2 seems a bit overwhelming the first time, but in reality it doesn’t take that long to do all the checks. But this part made us abort our first play, because we realized that we were playing it wrong (it should be hard to earn money, and we had loads of it ), and play it again. After that it turned out to a balanced game. The player with the biggest population wins the game.

There is luck involved in this one, as it is depending on what shows up on the market. But it isn’t a bad kind of luck. You will continuously have to adjust your strategy to let your city grow bigger. The interaction in this game is very minimal, as you’re building your own city, and besides checking the other players tiles if they would effect your newly build tile, there is no interaction. Despite the frustration from the bad die-cutting and the sometimes unclear rules (in both English and German), enjoyed the game very much. I ended my Essen 2013 recommendation for this game with the words: ‘Of course, I might be way wrong.’

I was wrong.

RATING: 8.5
My new #1 game from the past few years. Maybe it's from all the hours of playing Sim City/2000 growing up that city-building games always seem to strike my fancy. Plus, I love the dynamic of everyone's suburbs affecting one another, and in so doing, meaning that even though a tile might help you, it helps another even more, so you'd just better turn it into a lake. Hugely replayable. Even though there are a lot of things to keep track of from turn to turn, this is a much more gamer's tile-laying game than others like Carcassone or Prosperity. Unlike those games, you can envision the story of your town with a lot of clarity because of the synergy between different tiles. It's a must have.

RATING: 8
We purchased this game a few months ago and have enjoyed it immensely. The gameplay was new to us and it's quite fun to build out your town and make strategic decisions about what tiles you pick and where to place them. The gameplay is so compelling that I heard some rumors that it had influenced the latest version of the popular Civilization series of computer games, where purpose-built tile improvements have particular adjacency bonuses like they do in this game. Whether or not there was inspiration, it's clearly a well-liked mechanic. We did find that it's possible in the early game for a player to gain an insurmountable advantage; that hasn't happened too often though so we haven't been too bothered by it.

RATING: 3
I Have only played the 2 player version of the game and would like to play with more players to see if that changes some of the games dynamics. Not a huge fan of the objectives as the public ones are all-pay-bid-auctions, and the privet ones can be randomly shut down by other players just taking good tiles that you NEED for it. 
Mix in that there just doesn't feel like there is enough restaurants, airports, or other symbol tiles in the game to support tiles like the farms and slaughterhouses and the game just feels unpolished and unbalanced.
I quite enjoy the game for a comfy time, and as long as you don't care about final score and just want to make a cool town its OK, but for anything else it just fails to deliver and is poorly designed.

****UPDATE****
After more play through of the game I have to lower the rating as the flaws I mentioned above were even more impactful than I originally thought. ON top of that there was a very strong strategy that was discovered where you chain Heavy Factories in a straight line at the beginning of the game allowing you to take landfills and other unwanted tiles with only one connection ever, while still being able to support a hub of what ever else you need to make your other engines work, without ever needing to make lakes.
on top of that there are a larger number of tiles that can support a full yellow strategy in the game than just about any other color, and most of those tiles are also yellow.
